She learned how to interview victims of injustice – not only to listen, but to
encourage them to tell their stories. This is a very difficult skill that many
reporters never acquire, because victims project pain, confusion and fear. We
will say more about this in our chapter on interviewing.


She learned how to create and administer surveys, and to interpret the
results.


She learned how to solicit sensitive information from individuals engaged in
unethical or illegal activity. (She did so, in essence, by asking officials if
victims were telling the truth when they said that they were forced to pay
bribes. Instead of accusing them, she asked, “Why do you need this money?”
Their answer was that they could not afford to pay the costs of delivering court
judgments on their own, and bribes were their only recourse. Though surely
not the sole explanation, this reply may indeed be true, and requires reform.
Thus Allain learned not to judge individuals before she has heard their story.)


Not least, she gained confidence in her own ability to undertake and finish a
complex project. That confidence enabled her to withstand pressure from
authorities after her story was published. She thus learned how to defend her
values and her work.


You must make one further point to your students before closing the discussion and
the session: Before undertaking an investigation, ask yourself if you want to become
expert on the milieu where the story takes place. If students do not care about legal
issues, they will never find the energy to investigate the justice system. If they do not
care about food issues, they will be terribly bored by stories about agriculture.


_To make this point more clearly, you may ask students: What do you care about? What_
_do you want to know about it, that you do not already know?_


3 2


They do not need to answer that question fully, or finally, during the class. But they
must always keep it in mind. If there is nothing they care about deeply, nothing that
arouses their curiosity, they will have a very difficult time finding the commitment
necessary for an investigation. It is better that they discover this condition of success
in your class, now, than later in the field.


Please note that they do not need to care first and foremost about being an
investigator. They do need to care about something that they might
investigate.


3 3


Chapter 4

### **Creating** **hypotheses**

**/ 2 X 1.5 hours**


Advance reading:
For the instructor and the students:
Chapter 2 (pp. 13-26) from: Mark Lee Hunter et al., _Story-Based-Inquiry. A_
_manual for investigative journalists, UNESCO, 2009_


For the instructor:
Part One, chapter I (pp. 9-15) from: Luuk Sengers and Mark Lee Hunter, _The_
_Hidden Scenario. Plotting and outlining investigative stories,_ Centre for Investigative Journalism/Logan Handbooks, London, 2012.


3 5


**Introduction:**


**The key tool of contemporary investigative reporting: creating hypotheses**


From the 1970s until roughly the year 2000, investigative journalism had not
yet been conceptualised as a method. In the past decade this situation
changed dramatically. It is now understood that investigative reporters
employ methods, and that their methods distinguish them from other
reporters. _1_


The most basic of those methods is the one that you are now going to teach
your students: The use of hypotheses to frame and guide investigative
research. This method emerged from within the Global Investigative
Journalism Network, soon after its founding in 2003. Instead of a theoretical
construct, it is derived from field research and practice, and embodies the
solution to a practical difficulty: _We cannot investigate efficiently unless we_
_know what we are investigating, and what we are looking for._ Otherwise we will
either pile up information until we drown in it, or wander around helplessly
with no idea of where to find the information we need. Therefore, we need an
idea of what a _story might be_, before we can investigate whether or not it really
occurred that way, or in another way.


In other words, we need a hypothesis: “a supposition or proposed explanation
made on the basis of limited evidence as a starting point for further
investigation.” _2_


In this section you will be shown, in detail, how the method works, and how
to explain it to your students. Subsequently, you will help your students to
form investigative hypotheses of their own. These hypotheses, in turn, will
frame and orient their first investigations under your guidance. They will thus
provide you with a means to gauge your students’ progress – in other words,
a project management tool.


_1_
_See Casebook, Introduction, pp. 7-8, 9-10._


_2_
_d_ _h_ _O_ _f_ _d_ _h_ _//_ _f_ _dd_ _/d_ _fi_ _/_ _l_ _h/h_ _h_


**A. Explaining the use of hypotheses in journalism**


Consider a scientist who seeks to verify or disprove the origin of an epidemic. The
scientist first assesses the available facts – say, how the current events resemble
past epidemics the scientists has witnessed or read about, where the current
outbreak appears to have begun, where it appeared next, how it may have travelled
between those places – and creates a hypothesis that accounts for these facts. The
scientist then seeks to verify specific elements of the hypothesis, while seeking for
new facts.


The scientist accepts that the hypothesis may change as new facts emerge. The
hypothesis exists to be tested and verified, even disproved, so that a better
explanation may take its place. It is not a final truth; it is only a tool that enables him
or her to approach the truth.


In the same way, as investigative journalists we create a hypothesis – _a story that we_
_have reason to believe might be true_ - and then set out to verify that story. As we
proceed, we will discover either that our initial idea was more or less accurate, or we
will uncover evidence that points to another story.


Many journalists have difficulty understanding this approach. They would argue
that one cannot ethically say _what happened_ until one knows all the facts. That is
true, but only in a very limited sense. Of course one cannot tell the _final_ version of a
story without finding and assessing the pertinent facts. But you can nonetheless use
your _imagination_, based on what you have heard, read and experienced about the
subject, to deduce what may have occurred, and then test your deduction. Besides:
it is very, very hard to investigate a story by compiling an enormous number of facts,
and then trying to make sense of them. The usual result is drowning in a chaotic sea
of details. In order to investigate efficiently, we need a provisional idea of what we
are investigating.


Often journalists create hypotheses in a reflexive, unconscious way. Such a
journalist will go to the place where a story is happening with a preconceived
opinion, or even more than one opinion, about what he or she will find. If the
journalist is lazy or careless, he or she will then proceed to collect only the facts that
support the initial idea, and leave it at that. If the journalist is lucky, no one will be
injured as a result, beyond the indirect damage of misinforming the public about a
more or less important matter.


The outcome can be far richer if the journalist accepts that the initial idea may be
false, and is willing to replace it with one that better fits the facts. For example,
Thomas Friedman has said that when he investigated the massacres at Sabra and
Chatilla during the 1982 Israeli invasion of Lebanon, part of him wanted to prove
that the Israelis were responsible, and part of him wanted to prove that they were
not. Both of these initial ideas are hypotheses. They cannot be equally true; one _3_
must be false. But either of them is a good starting point for an investigation,
because one of them _might_ be true. Moreover, both of these hypotheses are useful,
because they focus the reporter’s attention on a key question: How did the massacre
come about?


This is of crucial importance, because otherwise the reporter may concentrate solely
on the carnage around him, and ignore its cause. It is also important because if we
are not looking for something, we are less likely to find it. In the end, Friedman
concluded that the Israelis were indeed responsible, and the result was a report that
earned him a Pulitzer Prize. _4_


_3_
Friedman, Thomas L., 1989, From Beirut to Jerusalem, New York: Doubleday, pp. 162-163


_4_


3 6


From our standpoint as journalists, a hypothesis is what we think may have
happened in a given situation. By verifying the hypothesis, we find new information
that gives us a truer idea of what happened. We then change the hypothesis to take
this new information into account. In the end, the verified hypothesis and its
supporting data become the core of the story.


**B. Standards for making hypotheses**


Any useful hypothesis must meet several tests:


1. _It takes the best information we have into account._


A hypothesis is not mere speculation. For example, we do not hypothesise the
existence of extra-terrestrial beings to explain how the Pyramids were constructed,
on the grounds that human beings alone are incapable of such majestic work. (This
was actually the key thesis of a best-selling book.) Nor do we look at an event and
say, “This can only be the work of a conspiracy,” when we have no concrete
information to suggest that a conspiracy was involved.

We do not invent facts to explain what we cannot see. We create a theory that
accounts for what we already know and what we can see. Then we deduce the facts
that might explain what we do not know, and seek to verify them.


For example, suppose that after a hard rain, houses on a city hillside collapsed. We
already know that in principle, houses are built in accord with building regulations.


**We then hypothesise:**


_The regulations were not observed, either by the builders of the houses, or by_
_the inspectors who certified that the houses were properly built, or both;_


_Or, the regulations are deficient, because they do not take into account_
_exceptional conditions like torrential rains._


2. _The hypothesis must contain assertions (or terms) that can be verified._


Your students almost surely suffer from a very common misconception among
journalists – the belief that in order to obtain valuable information, it suffices to call
someone on the telephone and ask them to reveal the truth. To demonstrate the
typical absurdity of this tactic, ask one of your students to pretend to be a building
inspector, then ask: “Did you accept a bribe to certify that these awful buildings were
safe?” The student may say, to be funny, “Of course, I always do!” To which you may
reply: “Thank you, I am going to put you in jail, unless you are killed first by relatives
of the victims.”


This is the point: No one of any intelligence volunteers information to a reporter
simply because the reporter asks for it. However, many sources will confirm or deny
information that is already in the reporter’s possession. By creating a hypothesis, we
see clearly what information we must acquire before we speak with anyone. These
facts will make us more powerful when we proceed to interviews.


3 7


Students may object that a reporter can sometimes gather critical information
without prior investigation – in particular, by questioning witnesses or victims at the
scene of a catastrophe or crime. That is certainly true. But witnesses to the collapse
of buildings will probably not have knowledge of or access to information that will be
very crucial in coming days, as we move beyond reporting the disaster to
understanding its causes. For example, they are not likely to be experts in building
regulations.


Our hypothesis tells us that we should take into account building regulations as well
as weather conditions. While our competitors are reporting official reactions of the
disaster and interviewing survivors and rescue crews, we can get ahead of them by
using open sources:


How much rain fell? (We can get this information from whoever collects
weather data.)


How steep is the hillside? (We can go to the scene and look at the slope.)


Who owns the houses? Who built them? (Of course, neighbours may be able
to tell us the name of the owner or builder. However, we can also obtain
information on the owner from property records. If there are no records, then
we have already discovered evidence that the building is illegal. Which raises
the questions: Why was the construction allowed? By whom?)


What do building regulations say about ensuring that foundations are safe?
(We can obtain building rules from the library or online.)


Who enforces them? (This too is in a library or online.)

Were the houses inspected? (We will find out if inspection records are public.)


Our hypothesis must enable us to understand which facts we can verify, and how,
or it is of no use. If our hypothesis states only, “The buildings fell down because
inspectors were paid bribes,” then we are focused only on whether or not we can find
a witness to that transaction. The hypothesis is therefore useless, because it does
not lead us to sources of information that enable us to advance in the absence of
confidential information. No one with direct knowledge of how bribes were paid is
going to speak to a reporter, because those sources must be involved in the crime.


3. In its initial form, the hypothesis must be written in three sentences or less.


Students love to show off the facts they have heard or discovered for themselves, and
will present them as if they were a story. Unfortunately, facts are not stories. A story
combines facts into a sequence of events that leads to a meaning. It can, and must,
be easily summarised. Otherwise your students do not understand what they are
trying to prove.


The instructor will find it necessary to remind students of this rule more than once.
The rule is not important in itself. What matters is that students acquire the habit of
thinking in terms of stories. If they think you only want them to find facts, they will
try to please you by pouring buckets of facts over your head every time they see you.
You must make it clear to them that you do not want only facts. You want stories,
which begin with hypotheses that are supported by facts. If there is no story, the
facts do not matter.


3 8


The instructor can ask, _Why must a hypothesis be written in only three sentences?_


Students will typically reply, “Because that’s the headline.” Others will say, “Because
otherwise no one will understand it.”


Both of these answers have something in common: They involve an audience. But
before a story makes sense to an audience, it must be meaningful to the person who
is telling it. We repeat: If students cannot express their hypothesis in a few
sentences, they do not understand it themselves. Put another way, they do not
understand what they are looking for, and how they might look for it.


**C. Creating hypotheses**


In this part of the class, your students will propose hypotheses for investigation.
**This is a crucial moment, because from now on, your students are engaged in**
**project work. You should make it clear that they are now designing their own**
**investigations** .


The exercise proceeds as follows:


1. Instruct the students to come up with a hypothesis (or story) that they would like
to investigate, in one short sentence (for example, “fraudulent schools are taking
money from the poor for worthless diplomas”). You can help them by asking: “What
do you think is going on?” Or: “What do you suspect?” And encourage them to start
their answer with “I think…” I think is a perfect introduction to a theory. (“I think” is
how Charles Darwin started his notes for On the Origin of Species.) You may tell
them that they need not be concerned about whether the hypothesis is perfectly
formed; they will learn how to sharpen a hypothesis through the class discussion.


**a.**


**b.**


**c.**


One after the other, as the discussion continues, write each hypothesis on top
of a blackboard or in a Word-file that you project on a screen.


Ask the class to vote for the three or four most interesting topics – and in
particular, those that they can investigate with the time and means available
to students. (Penetrating criminal gangs undercover does not qualify, but
someone always proposes it. If they do, please inform them that they can
undertake that project outside of class, but not for credit.) Inform them that
they will work on these topics in groups.

Choose one of the hypotheses. Directly under it, write the question: _What do_
_you hope to discover?_ Explain to the class that every investigation leads to
discoveries. What discoveries do they hope to reveal in their story?


The answer to this question – what do you hope to discover? – is a preliminary
hypothesis. For example, we have heard students say: _Landlords are cheating us on_
_rental apartments, and we want to discover how they do it, and how to stop them from_
_getting away with it._


That is a good start, but we need to go further – to turn the preliminary hypothesis
into a focused research tool. The following steps will achieve that goal.


3 9


**D. Ask whether their topic began with:**


a tip from a source (such as a student who was cheated).


a rumour


an official statement (for example, a warning from a university official that
some landlords are cheaters)


“leftovers” in a story (e.g. questions left unanswered in a media report about
students who were cheated )


an observation of the journalist (for example, he or she met a landlord who
tried to cheat). This is another way of asking: _What do you already know?_


**E. Ask the students to specify the facts that were**
**provided by the original source.**


For example, if it was a rumour, what exactly did the rumour claim? Please
note that the manual _Story-Based Inquiry_ contains a detailed example of a
story that began with a rumour: “Doctors are killing prematurely-born babies
to stop them from growing up with handicaps.” If you wish, you can use this
example to illustrate the point: **What matters about a rumour is whether it**
**can be verified.**


If the topic comes from leftovers, what facts did the initial story contain?


If it is an observation, what exactly did the journalist observe?


Finally, ask the students if the case at hand reminds them of related stories
or facts. For example, did a similar event occur in another place? Or is the
current event a repeat of one that happened a while ago?


As the discussion proceeds, make sure that the students take notes to capture it.
Tell them, “We are defining your project. You must not waste this work.” Inform them
that at the end of the discussion they must furnish you with their notes, and that
you will give a grade to the notes before returning them.  Otherwise, you will surely
observe that they are merely enjoying the discussion, without capturing the
knowledge it creates. That knowledge pertains to their stories, but also to the method
they are supposed to be learning.


4 0


**F. When students have provided a description of**
**what they hope to discover, and the facts currently**
**at their disposal, say:** _This is what we know._
_What else would we like to know, that we don’t know yet?_
_What questions do you have about the story?_


Ask one student to write their questions in a list that everyone can read in real time
(on a blackboard or projected Word-file). Encourage the students to start every
question with “ _How…_ ” We think it is better to focus students on _how_ things occur,
than on _why_ they occur. The “how” points to concrete, verifiable actions, while the
“why” ends with unverifiable motives.


The hypothesis: _Landlords are cheating us on rental apartments_ led to these
questions:


How does the racket work?


How is the university involved?


How can this be stopped?


**G. Now ask your students to** _**answer these questions,**_
**using their imagination. Point out that every answer is**
**hypothetical, and, indeed, part of a hypothesis.**
**Encourage them also to start every answer with a human**
**subject, followed by an active verb. We find this a useful**
**‘trick’ to provide concrete answers - and concreteness is**
**what we need, if we will ever be able to prove the story.**


In the case of the cheating landlords, students came up with this extended
hypothesis:


_Landlords demand exorbitant rents and large security deposits that are never_
_returned, for rooms that are in terrible condition. The landlords advertise and use_
_university housing services to find renters. The university makes no attempt to identify_
_cheating landlords, or to sanction them, and provides no help to cheated students._


Point out to your students that they have just created a list of concrete elements that
may play a role in their story – rents, deposits, rooms, landlords, advertisements,
etc.


4 1


**H. Now ask the group to specify which of the**
**different points in their hypothesis is easiest to verify.**


Many students will approach their topic from the opposite direction – that is, they
will focus on a secret that can only be explored through a heroic adventure, like
penetrating a criminal gang. Please inform them that every investigation becomes
more difficult as it proceeds; if they cannot do the easy work first, they will never be
able to do the hard work.


At this point in the discussion, put their hypothesis in view again. Tell them you will
read it to them slowly, and ask them to stop you every time that they hear something
that can be verified. Then, ask them how they will verify that point.

In the preceding example:


We can verify that landlords demand exorbitant rents and deposits by
comparing student rents with market prices for other renters.


If landlords advertise to students, we can locate these advertsements in media
(such as magazines or billboards) that are targeted at students.


We can also locate students who have complained about landlords to
university housing services, with no result.


Of these three verifications, the easiest will be to find rental prices and landlord
advertisements, because they are open sources. The hardest task will be to locate
students who complained, and to confirm that the university does nothing about the
complaints.


4 2


**I. Concluding remarks**


Once your students have created a viable investigative hypothesis – a story line that
contains elements that can be verified, including from open sources – you may want
to make the following points:


1. Making a hypothesis seems time consuming. But with practice, it can be done in
minutes. The time lost when you have no hypothesis is measured in days, weeks and
months. The larger point is that news reporters are trained to do everything fast, and
that is their default mode. Investigators must be less reactive, and more pro-active;
they must take control of the story from the start.


2. At some point in the session, a student will ask: “I would like to keep an open
mind. Won’t a hypothesis increase the chance that I miss something important, by
focusing me on one set of facts?”


In fact, a hypothesis can blind a reporter. This usually occurs in two ways:


A reporter turns up evidence that contradicts the hypothesis, but refuses to
consider that evidence;


The reporter is using the hypothesis in an unconscious or reflexive way, in
order to make the work faster and easier. Typically, the reporter has a
preconceived idea, and a minimum of evidence to support it, and is happy
with that state of affairs.


You can alert your students to these dangers, and offer a solution: An honest
reporter constantly tests his or her hypothesis against the facts that emerge in the
investigation.

In the following sessions, we will show students how to create a timeline and a
source map. These two tools will then become the basis for a “masterfile” – a
database that the student can use as a working asset throughout his or her career.


4 3


Chapter 5

### **Using** **timelines**

**/ 2 X 1.5 hours**


Advance reading for the instructor:


Luuk Sengers and Mark Lee Hunter, _The Hidden Scenario. Plotting and outlin-_
_ing investigative stories_, Centre for Investigative Journalism/Logan, London,


For the class:


Chapter 3 (“Using the Open Doors: Background and Deduction”) from Story-Based Inquiry.


**Introduction:**


**The key tool of contemporary investigative reporting: creating hypotheses**


After the hypothesis, the timeline is the most important tool used by
investigators, and probably the most widely used. It helps us to make sense of
complex matters, by revealing causality and consequences of related events.
It shows us what is missing from our story—that is, what might have
happened before and after events that we have confirmed – and keeps the
information we have gathered in a clear, strong order. A timeline also creates
a foundation for the simplest and strongest narrative structures, as we will
see later. In fact, the timeline enables us to begin creating our narrative while
we are still conducting research.


For all these reasons, you must insist that your students learn how to create,
enrich and maintain a timeline. In successive sessions, you will show them:


1. How to identify specific events related to the hypothesis;


2. How to deduce and verify other events that must have occurred;


3. How to compile these events, and their supporting documentation, into a
database.


You can tell your students:


_In order to verify our hypothesis, we need to understand:_
_What specific events may have taken place;_
_What evidence, or documentation, those events created;_
_How we may obtain that evidence._


You may also tell them: _To do this, we need to use our imaginations_ . In the rest
of the session we will encourage them to do just that. We recommend that you
read _The Hidden Scenario_ for further insight into the techniques
recommended in this chapter; they had not been developed when _Story-Based_
_Inquiry_ was published.


4 5


**A. Defining a sequence of events**


Start by asking: _Who can explain what a “timeline” is?_


Answer: A timeline (or chronology) is a list of events that follows the order in which
they occurred. In a timeline, one event follows another.


You can tell your students: _Every hypothesis contains a timeline, because it refers to_
_a sequence of events._


You may now take one of the hypotheses that students created in previous sessions
and write it on top of a blackboard or Word-file that you project.


Ask your students to take their hypothesis apart; to identify the seperate events that
make up their hypothesis, and put these events in a list. They can easily identify the
separate events by counting the verbs in the hypothesis: every verb is the expression
of a different action.


Please be sure, before you do it in front of them, that you have identified the number
of verbs in the hypothesis, and the events to which they refer.


To make the exercise clear, we will show you how it works with the hypothesis that
students created. We urge you very strongly to create your own examples from
local events. You will know better than we do what stories are of interest to your
students. Here is our example from the previous chapter:


_Landlords demand exorbitant rents and large security deposits that are never_
_returned, for rooms that are in terrible condition. The landlords advertise and_
_use university housing services to find renters. The university makes no attempt_
_to identify cheating landlords, or to sanction them, and provides no help to_
_cheated students._


Notice that this hypothesis refers to a number of events. The events show up in the
hypothesis every time that a verb appears, because verbs signify action:


1. Landlords demand exorbitant rents and large security deposits.


2. Landlords decline to return this money to students.


3. Landlords advertise for renters.


4. Landlords use university housing services to find renters.


5. University housing services help the landlords find renters.


6. University housing services tolerate cheating landlords.


7. University housing services deny help to cheated students.


4 6


Now let us remake this list according to the chronological order in which the events
must have occurred. First, landlords must find renters:


1. Landlords advertise for renters.


2. Landlords use university housing services to find renters.


3. University housing services help the landlords find renters.


Now the landlords take advantage of the renters:


4. Landlords demand exorbitant rents and large security deposits.


5. Landlords never return this money to students.


Finally, the university allows them to get away with it:


6. University housing services tolerate cheating landlords.


7. University housing services deny help to cheated students.


Now ask your students: _What actions must occur before and after these events?_ If we
focus on the consequences of our events, we may imagine that:


8. Landlords continue to dupe more students.


9. Students sue landlords with the help of lawyers.


Already, in our hypothetical sequence of events, each event enables the next.


Landlords advertise and use university services in order to find students
whom they can cheat.


By declining to sanction cheaters, the university enables the scheme to
prosper at the expense of students.


Finally, some students strike back through lawsuits.


The class will be much more lively if you perform this exercise using hypotheses
provided by students. (You will require them to apply this procedure to their own
hypotheses in any case.) Whatever hypothesis you use, follow the procedure
described above:


Set out the list of events contained in the hypothesis.


Put those events in the probable chronological order in which they occurred.


Imagine other plausible events that must have occurred, either to enable
known events, or as a consequence of those events.


4 7


Some of the events that they propose will be of less interest than others. Do not be
concerned about that for the moment. Instead, concentrate on getting your students
to elaborate a rich timeline between the moment when the story begins, and the
moment that it generates newsworthy consequences. When the imagination runs
dry, or the timeline contains far-off or implausible events, it is time to stop. Ideally a
timeline consists of between 5 and 10 important events.


**B. Formulate the events as concretely as possible**


Point out to the students that the next step will be to verify the events in their
timeline. They must therefore formulate every event concretely, so they know exactly
what they are trying to verify.


It helps to begin every sentence with a human subject. It is easier to
investigate human beings than abstract problems or systems. Moreover,
at some point, students will need human sources to verify the events. In
short, students should ask not “What do I want to investigate?” but
“Who do I want to investigate?”


Make sure that the character in the event is engaged in an activity. Only
activity leaves traces that we can follow up to prove that something
happened. “Landlords cheat” is less easy to prove than “Landlords
demand exorbitant rents and large security deposits.” “Demanding” is a
real action (the landlord is talking to the student), while “cheating” is an
abstract summary, and a subjective opinion at that.


Finally, urge your students to stay clear of negatives and predictions.
For instance, the statement above, “Landlords never return the money
to students” states what does not happen. Things that don’t happen are
hard to prove, so let’s change the sentence to state what does happen
instead: “Landlords refuse to return the money to the students.”
Likewise it is impossible to prove something that has not happened yet

      - for example, “Landlords who cheat form a syndicate to control the
market.” Perhaps they will, but at the moment, this is speculation, not
hypothesis.


**C. Identify the maximum and minimum story**


As a general case, your initial hypothesis, and the timeline that you derive from it,
will contain very few verified facts. (In the example used for The Hidden Scenario,
concerning toxic chemicals that are used to manufacture water pipes, only one fact
was known at the outset: scientists had discovered that plastic water pipes leak
chemicals into drinking water.) In the example above, we know only two facts, which
we have learned through tips from students:


Landlords demand exorbitant rents and large security deposits.


Landlords never return this money to students.


4 8


4 6


Every other “fact” in our timeline is hypothetical, and remains to be verified. This will
surely recur when you work with students’ hypotheses, and it will worry some of
them. They may object: _How do we know that this or that event occurred?_ If they do
not ask, you may pose the question yourself.


Your answer: _Some of these events are only hypotheses, which we will verify. They are_
_what we think must have happened. If they did not, we will discover what really_
_happened when we attempt to verify them._


The students may then ask: _What if our hypotheses are not true? Do we still have a_
_story?_


Whether or not they ask, we need to address this question, too. The answer is:


_The hypothesis should be framed to reveal the most important story we can find here,_
_without merely speculating. We need to know what the most important story may be,_
_so that we can decide whether or not it is worth exploring. If the most important story_
_we can hypothesize from the available facts is neither very interesting nor very easy,_
_we abandon it._


In the case above, students might suggest that university officials are being bribed
by landlords. You may add this event to your timeline; however, you may also note
that it is not necessary to go that far in order to have an interesting story. In fact, you
may point out that one of the common, and gravest, errors made by investigative
reporters is to make accusations that their evidence cannot fully support, in order to
make the story appear more dramatic. _1_


We faced this exact situation in one of our classes: A student hypothesized that
university housing officials were taking bribes from students desperate to find an
apartment. She became expert in how the housing office functioned, but found no
bribes – only a multitude of example of how students manipulated a broken system.
She thought that she had failed, because she could not prove that housing officials
were criminals. Instead, she had proven that their office was completely
dysfunctional. Once she realised that this was a valuable story, she wrote it and
published it.


**E. Take-home assignment: Creating a timeline**


Before the next class session, your students should repeat the above procedures for
every one of their hypotheses, creating a timeline. Make clear to them that this work
must be done as a word processing file, and not merely freehand. Explain to them
that before long, this preliminary work will expand into a database.


_1_
_A classic example is “The Uncounted Enemy”, a CBS Reports documentary broadcast on January 23, 1982. The_
_documentary charged that American generals and their staffs falsified intelligence reports to show that they were_
_reducing the number of enemy personnel during the Vietna War. The network was sued for libel; the case did not_
_come to trial, but CBS soon lost its libel insurance, and the aggressivity of its reporting visibly declined._
_A_ _good_ _brief account of the incident can be found on Wikipedia: http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_ _Uncounted_ _Enemy._


Chapter 6

### **Investigative** **reporting** **after Watergate**

**/ 1.5 hours**


Advance reading for the instructor and class:


Story-Based Inquiry, Chapter 3, “Verification”


Lina Joudy, “Gharghesia archeological site disappears
under village in East Syria”


5 1


**A. Introductory lecture: How document**
**searches define investigative reporting**


Following the resignation of Richard M. Nixon from the US Presidency in 1975,

investigative reporters bathed in “exaltation.” _[1]_ They were folk heroes and models for

other journalists, in the US as well as other countries. _[2]_ Not least, the reaction to
Watergate made clear that investigation is hardly “good old-fashioned journalism,
properly done”. Other journalists at the time regarded investigators as “inquisitors”
(an allusion to medieval Spanish torturers of the Catholic Church) whose success in

obtaining confidential documents was due entirely to “inside” human sources. _[3]_


This was flattering, and it was also nonsense. No one is obliged to talk to a journalist
under threat of torture, and journalists do not need inside sources to obtain a great
many documents. In this session we are going to show students that they can obtain
significant information before they talk to anyone at all.


1. The institutionalization of document-based reporting


Your students need to understand the true relevance of the post-Watergate
investigative journalism movement to their own skills and careers: Journalists
realized that instead of simply recording what official sources told them, they could
conceive and document a story on their own. They could thus be critical in a more
powerful and positive way than by stating an opinion. Instead, they could influence
society through obtaining and publishing facts that moved individuals and
institutions to action. (Stubborn students may again observe that lies may have the
same effect. This is true, but if they wish to spend their lives lying, they are in the
wrong place.) This insight had two important methodological implications:


The focus of their work shifted from gaining access to powerful figures to
confronting powerful figures with facts.


Reporters were thereby forced to become expert at finding and
documenting facts before, not after, they asked questions.


_1_
_David Gelman, "Jugular Journalism?" Newsweek, May 10 1976, pp. 79-80._


_2_
_Trezise, James, James Glen Stovall, and Hamid Mowlana. Watergate, a Crisis for the World:_
_A Survey of British and French Press Reaction Toward an American Political Crisis. Pergamon Press, 1980._


_3_
_Carla Maria Rupp, "Investigative Journalists Reveal How They Do It," Editor and Publisher, Jan. 11, 1975._


Donald L. Bartlett and James B. Steele were reporters at the Philadelphia Inquirer
who greatly accelerated this shift, in a Pulitzer-Prize winning investigation of
racially-biased justice in Philadelphia (1972). Their report involved reviewing literally
thousands of pages of court judgments and transcripts, as well as interviewing
victims and officials of the system. They urged other journalists to develop “a
document state of mind”— to always think of what documents might be available to
support their stories — and they were right.


The creation of Investigative Reporters and Editors (IRE) Inc. in 1975 provided
journalists with a platform to diffuse such best practices. IRE’s awards, conferences,
publications, and training seminars set a standard for the practice of investigative
reporting and publicised the necessary methods. In the view of investigative
journalism historian James L. Aucoin, IRE was a key reason that investigation

changed from a specialty to a broadly-based “social practice” in the US. _[4]_
Document-based research was the core of that social practice.


Two signs of the progression of this movement were that environmental _[5]_ and
business news, previously regarded as graveyards of the profession, became
dynamic sectors for ambitious reporters. In 1979, a survey of US business editors at
newspapers selling more than 50,000 copies daily showed that two-thirds had

published investigations in the preceding year. _[6]_ At some US journals business

investigations doubled during the 1970s. _[7]_ Thus investigative reporting techniques
that originated in political exposés spread constantly into new sectors, until they

were applied to every corner of public life, from business to zoos. _[8]_


_4_
_See James L. Aucoin, The Evolution of American Investigative Journalism.University of Missouri Press, 2005._


_5_
_Environmental_


_6_
_Ernest C. Hynds, "Business Coverage is Getting Better," Journalism Quarterly 57:297-304._


_7_
_J.T.W. Hubbard, "Business News in Post-Watergate Era," Journalism Quarterly 53:488-493._


_8_
_IRE’s archives, compiled from both winning and losing entries in the organisation’s annual awards competition,_
_illustrate how students can approach a wide range of subjects.The instructor can access the archives by joining IRE_
_at: http://www.ire.org/membership/_


5 2


5 3


The example of Watergate in the US led to similar movements on an international
scale. In France, for example, investigative journalism spread in the 1980s from
exposés of political corruption to revelations of deadly policies in the health system,

and even crime in the national museums. _[9]_ In Kenya, after developing investigative
capacity in political reporting beginning in the 1990s, the Nation Media Group

successfully used those capacities to launch _Business Daily_ in 2009. _[10]_


Since the launch of the Global Investigative Journalism Network (GIJN) at

Copenhagen in 2001, _[11]_ national and regional non-profit investigative journalism

foundations have multiplied around the world. _[12]_ The instructor may gain great
insight to this movement by spending some time on the GIJN website, www.gijn.org.
There are currently nearly 100 such centers. In many of these regions, from the
former Soviet republics to the Arab world, there was virtually no investigative
reporting previously. In all of these places and all of these cases, searching for
documents – reports, memoranda, articles, video clips, and so on – became the
foundation of professional practice for investigative reporters.


The GIJN was created during one of the lulls in the cycles that characterize
investigative journalism, and it was a highly dangerous moment. The generation of
reporters who began their activity in the 1970s, inspired by Watergate, were now in
their 50s. They had succeeded in making investigative reporting a global practice,
but they had been less effective at training a new generation to take their places. It
was thus very fortunate that in Eastern Europe, a new cohort of investigative
reporters, led by Paul Radu and Stefan Candea of the Romanian Centre for
Investigative journalism and Alexenia Dimitrova at the Bulgarian newspaper 24
Hours, emerged as global leaders. Radu, in particular, went on to become a leading
innovator of investigative methods through his work with the Organised Crime and
Corruption Reporting Project (OCCRP). Radu’s chief innovation was to combine
databases concerning different forms of property – the stock of publicly-traded firms,
or land and buildings – into powerful journalistic tools like the “Investigative
Dashboard.”


It is a historical fact that the Arab World became part of this movement through Arab
Reporters for Investigative Journalism (ARIJ), the sponsor of this curriculum. Alone
among Arab organisations, ARIJ took part in the GIJN nearly from its inception.
ARIJ also commissioned the investigative reporting manual “Story-Based Inquiry”,
and partnered with UNESCO to ensure its global distribution. The manual became a
fundamental teaching resource worldwide almost immediately on its publication in
2009. You may thus truthfully inform your students that the Arab World has had a
direct impact on shaping the global investigative journalism movement.


_9_
_Hunter, Le journalisme d’investigation, op. cit._


_10_
_Mark Lee Hunter and Luk N. Van Wassehove, “The Nation Media Group.” INSEAD case, forthcoming (2014)._


_11_
_For a brief history of the GIJN, see Casebook, pp. 7-8._


_12_
_The non-profit movement was largely inspired by the work of Charles L. Lewis, founder of the_

_fit_ _C_ _t_ _f_ _P_ _bli_ _I_ _t_ _it_ _(CPI) i_ _th_ _U_ _it_ _d St_ _t_


**B. A contemporary example from Syria**


Your students must be able to see that document-based investigative reporting
entails a competitive advantage, because most reporters are incapable or unwilling
of doing this work. Usually, reporters rely on documents provided to them by
someone else; they do not seek to obtain documents on their own. A reporter capable
of these practices will always find rich sources.


At a minimum, the instructor can instill respect for the determination and creativity
required to discover documentary evidence of true stories. At most, the instructor
can provide young professionals with a method that will serve them for the rest of
their lives.


Lina Joudy’s story, “Gharghesia archeological site disappears under village in East
Syria”, demonstrates documentary techniques in a very hard place for a reporter to
work. Her story details the destruction of a superb archaeological site in Syria, by
villagers who wished to use its stones in their own houses. The story begins with a
reference to a published book. Other books are cited as she proceeds. She also refers
to official documents, including a law, a 1977 government report, and a recent

decree. All of these documents could be obtained in a library. _[13]_ Your students should
be advised that Internet is not a substitute for the library.


The instructor may ask students to perform the following analysis of this story before
coming to class:


On one side of a sheet of paper, list the reporter’s documentary sources.


On the other side, list the human sources.


Now, draw a line connecting human sources to the documentary sources with
which they are probably familiar, or to which their activities are related. (Not
all human sources will be connected to documents.)


This exercise should demonstrate that the reporter’s documents clearly inform
and guide her use of human sources. The documents are connected to institutions
where her sources work. By knowing their contents, the reporter can engage in
detailed and precise discussions with experts and officials. Fort example, from the
start of her investigation, Lina Joudy can say to villagers: “There was a castle here.
Scholars wrote about it in detail. It is gone now. How did this happen?”


Ordinary reporters ask their sources, “What happened?” Investigative reporters can
also ask their sources: “Isn’t this what happened?” The second question is much
more powerful than the first. It shows the source that the reporter cannot easily be
lied to – at least, not as easily as most reporters can be lied to. It also makes the
source’s task, of answering the question, much easier. Sources do not like to explain
everything to reporters. Nor do they like to volunteer information that a reporter
clearly does not possess, and may not be capable of appreciating. However, sources
often confirm information that they would not volunteer. And they often enjoy
speaking with someone who has taken the trouble to learn something before asking
questions.


_13_
_In the first edition of The Reporter’s Handbook, author John Ullman tells a joke: Two angels are discussing how_
_to help humanity. One says, “Let’s build a place where journalists can find all the knowledge in the world,_

_d_ _it_ _t_ _h_ _l_ _l_ _” Th_ _th_ _“Y_ _t_ _id_ _It_ _l_ _d_ _i_ _t_ _It’_ _ll_ _d_ _lib_


5 4


**Annex to Chapter 4: Further landmarks of**
**investigative reporting**


In connection with this syllabus, members of the GIJN were asked in the summer of
2013 to suggest works that influenced their own ambitions and practice – works that
helped to define their own dreams. We reproduce the article that resulted below. The
original can be accessed at:
http://gijn.org/2014/02/05/resources-a-guide-to-investigative-books-and-films/


The first interest of this list is that it represents works which influenced the practice
of leading investigative reporters. Contributors include several authors included in
the Global Investigative Journalism Casebook.


Second, the instructor may wish to select an excerpt from one of the works below as
a student assignment. It is unrealistic for an instructor to expect of a student, let
alone oneself, to read all of these works in the short term. We recommend instead
that the instructor determine if one or another of these works is easily accessible, in
whole or part – for example, Amazon.com typically offers excerpts, or portions may
have been published as articles – and use whatever material the instructor finds
inspiring. Students may not share the instructor’s enthusiasm, just as immature
palates may not appreciate the best tea; so it is important to explain briefly why a
given work is exciting.


At a minimum, the instructor can use this list to make a key point to students:
Investigative reporters have examined a very wide range of subjects. There are not
limits to the domains where investigative methods may be used. Nor is investigative
reporting confined to a single country or culture. Great work has been done on every
continent.


More important, we urgently ask the instructor to use this list as a model for a
further selection of works in Arabic. Outside the US and the UK, little scholarship
has been dedicated to investigative reporting, and this applies to the Arab world. In
preparing this curriculum we asked Arab practitioners for their local models of
investigation, including fiction in the tradition of Upton Sinclair; few could think of
any. Scholarly research is needed into the regional tradition of reformist
documentary literature and film, and in particular into how regional journalists
overcame obstacles to investigative work. Contributors will be cited in future editions
of this syllabus.


5 5


**Lyra McKee, founder of The Muckraker (UK),**
**recommends:**


Check out Jeremy Scahill's _Dirty Wars_ and Woody Klein's _The Inside Stories of_
_Modern Political Scandals: How Investigative Reporters Have Changed the Course of_
_American History._


**Stephen Grey, Reuters journalist and author (UK),**
**recommends:**


_Merchants of Grain: The Power and Profits of the Five Giant Companies at the Center_
_of the World's Food Supply,_ by Dan Morgan (1976). Still not to be missed by anyone.


_The Box: How the Shipping Container Made the World Smaller and the World Economy_
_Bigger_ by Marc Levinson (2006). An essential investigation into the world trading
system; impossible to understand globalisation without this book.


On Criminal Justice:
_Anatomy of Injustice: A Murder Case Gone Wrong,_ by Ray Bonner (2012),
an unmissable look at the US death penalty system by one of the greats.


On War:


_- Neil Sheehan, A Bright Shining Lie (1988)_ offers a classic example of telling the big
story of a war by focussing on the tale of one man's contribution, Col John Vann.


_- Dispatches_ by Michael Herr (1977) is the story that inspired “Apocalypse Now”. War
writing at its best - and deadly insight.


_- The Politics of Heroin in Southeast Asia_ by Alfred W. McCoy is a subversive look at
the CIA's role in Vietnam. (This is deep dark, digging -- still don't know if it is all
true!). (“It’s a dated classic but explains in detail how the US Government moved
drugs through Laos and Cambodia during the Vietnam war,” comments UK
investigative reporter Andrew Weir. “It started out more as an anthropological study
but the fact that the author spent a lot of time in the region during the 1960s gives
it a unique credibility.”)


5 6


**Drew Sullivan, co-founder of the Organized Crime**
**and Corruption Reporting Project (Bosnia), recommends:**


_Endless Enemies_ by Jonathan Kwitny (1986). Here the late, great IRE member looked
at how US foreign policy was often self-defeating. The book covers various US led
coups, the Dulles brothers, The Times reporter in Iran working for the CIA and other
gems. Also, his book on the Nugan Hand Bank, _The Crimes of Patriots_ (1987) was a
classic.


_Godfather of the Kremlin_, by Paul Klebnikov (2000). A withering look at gangster
capitalism in Russia during the Yeltsin years. It especially tells the tale of the late
Boris Berezovsky.


In _Red Mafiya_ (2000) the late, great Robert Friedman singlehandedly reported on the
Russian mafia in the 90s, especially looking at money laundering through the Bank
of New York.


In _The Puzzle Palace_ by James Bamford (1982), he takes on probably the most
difficult subjects an investigative reporting can tackle – the top secret National
Security Agency. Yet he collects an amazing amount of information. It’s a pretty
impressive book and he had to fight like hell to get it published. He followed it up
with two more on the NSA, _A Pretext for War_ (2004) and _The Shadow Factory_ (2008),
which demonstrate the same reporting prowess.


Mark Lee Hunter, author, editor, and reporter (France), recommends:


_Le pull-Over_ Rouge by Gilles Perrault (1978). In a narrative that manages to be
breathless and crushing at once, Perrault shows that the last man guillotined in
France was caught between a one-sided official "investigation" and an incompetent
defender. Thanks to this book, the death penalty became history in France in 1981.


_Vicious Circles:_ The Mafia in the Marketplace, by Jonathan Kwitny (1979). A
masterpiece of insight into organised crime, written in a natural, compelling style.


David McClintick's _Indecent Exposure_ (1982) is not only a great narrative, it's the
great (nonfiction) business novel that all the literary critics were weeping and
whining for, but couldn't take from a reporter.


_L'Affaire du Sang_ by Anne-Marie Casteret (1992). The single greatest book, and
investigation, in the history of French journalism. Casteret proved that high officials
of the French State knowingly sold blood products contaminated with the AIDS virus
to hemophiliacs. Her revelations shattered the responsible government, and
follow-up reports still make the news. Masterful medicine-meets-crime reporting.


_Erin Brockovich_ (2000), a film by Steven Soderbergh starring Julia Roberts, does a
brilliant job of showing how to build source relationships with dreadfully victimized
people – and thus to attract other sources who know how they were victimized. There
is more tradecraft in this film than in All the President’s Men.


5 7


Margo Smit, director of the Dutch-Flemish investigative journalists’
association, VVOJ (Netherlands), recommends works in Dutch and
German:


Chris de Stoop's book on the traffic in women: _Ze zijn zo lief, meneer_ ('they are so
sweet, sir...'), from 1992. (Mark Lee Hunter comments: “A masterpiece that combines
undercover and documentary research into a stunning narrative of oppression and
injustice.”)


Here in the Netherlands a book that inspires my students at the moment is the story
of how ABN Amro (a Dutch bank) was nationalized back in 2008: _De Prooi_ (The Prey,
2009), written by Jeroen Smit, current professor of journalism at Rijksuniversiteit
Groningen. I consider it a must read.


Gerard Ryle’s book _Firepower_ : _The most spectacular fraud in Australian history_
(2009) was the predecessor to Offshore Leaks and a major work Down Under.


Eric Hennekam, VVOJ member and specialist in archival research
(Netherlands), recommends:


All the journalism work of Karl-Stig-Erland "Stieg" Larsson,
like _Sverigedemokraterna: den nationella rörelsen_ (2001).


_Legacy of Ashes_ : The History of the CIA, by Tim Weiner (2007).


Andrew Weir, investigative author and reporter (UK), recommends:

_The Quiet American_, by Graham Greene (1955). Fiction perhaps, but one of those
cases where fiction speaks truer than reportage.


Also Hunter Thompson's long-neglected piece of investigative reportage, _Hell’s_
_Angels: The Strange and Terrible Saga of the Outlaw Motorcycle Gangs (1966)._


Lise Olsen, investigative reporter at the Houston Chronicle (US),
recommends in English and Spanish:


I have used the excellent documentary “La Doble Desaparecida” (CNN in Spanish,
2003) to talk about investigative reporting – excellent example of the use of
documents and confrontational interviews and just an amazing tale. Available at
http://vimeo.com/38926180.


_Con la muerte en el bolsillo_ (2005) by Mexican investigative reporter and public
records expert Maria Idalia Gomez – a long-time investigator of attacks against
journalists for the IAPA - and her partner Dario Fritz is a great investigative book
about drug trafficking.


My absolute favorite investigative book in English is _The Looming Tower: Al Qaeda_
_and the Road to 9/11_ (2006). A tour de force of writing and reporting by Lawrence
Wright. Reading any of the amazing chapters and looking at the footnotes would be
instructive for any level of journalism study.


5 8


Mexican reporter Sandra Rodriguez’ investigative biography of a teenager in Juarez
who arranged the kidnapping/murder of his own sister and parents, and then tried
to blame it on organized crime and collect ransom, is full of facts and written in an
outstanding narrative style: _La Fábrica del Crimen_ (2012).


Serena Tinari, investigative reporter and filmmaker, recommends
works in English, Italian and French (Switzerland):


If you’re investigating Big Pharma, read these:


John Le Carré: _The Constant Gardener (2001)_


Merrill Goozner: _The $800 Million Pill: The Truth behind the Cost of a New Drug (2004)_


Marco Bobbio: _Giuro di esercitare la medicina in libertà e indipendenza (2004)_


Ray Moynihan and Alan Cassels: _Selling Sickness: How the World's Biggest_
_Pharmaceutical Companies Are Turning Us All into Patients (2005)_


Marcia Angell: _The Truth about the Drug Companies (2005)_


Jacky Law: _Big Pharma: Exposing the Global Healthcare Agenda (2006)_


Ray Moynihan and David Henry: _Disease Mongering (2006)_


Catherine Riva and Jean-Pierre Spinosa : _Pourquoi vaccine-t-on les jeunes filles contre_
_le cancer du col de l'utérus? La piqûre de trop (2010)_


Florence Graves, founding director of the Schuster Institute for
Investigative Reporting (US), recommends:


Brooke Kroeger's _Undercover Reporting_ : _The Truth About Deception_ (2012), which
makes the case for the importance of undercover reporting. See also the astonishing
companion database of hundreds of undercover stories that she created and made
available at New York University: http://dlib.nyu.edu/undercover/


Abdullah Vawda, executive director of the Forum for African
Investigative Reporters (South Africa), recommends:


_Killing for Profit: Exposing the Illegal Rhino Horn Trade_ by Julian Rademeyer (2012),
described correctly by Amazon as “a meticulous, devastating and revelatory account”
of wildlife trafficking.


5 9


Paul Lashmar, investigative journalist and professor at Brunel
University (UK), recommends:


Andrew Jennings’ excellent work on the global football association, FIFA, _notably_
_Foul: The Secret World of FIFA_ (2006).


Two of Robert Whitaker’s books are among the most cited in the critical psychiatry
literature: _Mad in America_ (2002), and _Anatomy of an Epidemic_ (2010). Whitaker’s
work has alerted both the medical community and the general public to the serious
dangers of psychotropic medicines when used for extended periods. He has become
a standard reference for the facts.


Ana Arana, director of Fundación MEPI (Mexico), recommends:


Jared Diamond's Pulitzer Prize winning book _Guns, Germs and Steel_ (1997) looks at
how environmental factors had a decisive influence on human history. (Mark Lee
Hunter comments: You can debate over whether or not this is investigative, because
it reveals no secrets. What it does do is use open sources – specifically, the scientific
literature from a stunning range of fields – to weave a convincing theory of how
human society evolved differently in Europe and elsewhere.)


Gabriel Garcia Marquez’s _News of a Kidnapping_ (1998) details the kidnapping,
imprisonment, and eventual release of a handful of prominent journalists and others
in Colombia in the early 1990s by the Medellin drug cartel, operated by the late Pablo
Escobar. (Mark Lee Hunter notes: “Marquez was a famous journalist before he
became a famous novelist. He remains a great reporter – an implicit answer to the
dumbbell notion that journalism necessarily corrupts artists.”)


From Latin America I would include _The Art of Political Murder_, by Francisco
Goldman (2008), on the murder of Bishop Juan Gerardi, Guatemala’s leading
human rights activist, who was bludgeoned to death in 1998, two days after the
presentation of a groundbreaking church-sponsored report implicating the military
in the murders and disappearances of some two hundred thousand civilians.


Anne Marie O'Connor’s _The Lady in Gold_ (2012) tells of the portrait of Adele
Bloch-Bauer, a Jewish Heiress and Gustav Klimt's muse and patron in
turn-of-the-century Vienna, its fate under Nazi rule, and the battle to recover it by
survivors in the US and Canada.


6 0


Luuk Sengers, author and reporter (Netherlands), recommends:


I remember the impact of yet another great book on raving young economy reporters
in the 90s: _Barbarians at the Gate: The Fall of RJR Nabisco_, by Bryan Burrough and
John Helyar (1990). It completely revolutionized the way we wrote about companies:
less focus on money and structures, and more on power and people.


Another book that should not be absent from our list is _Fast Food Nation_ : _The Dark_
_Side of the All-American Meal_ by Eric Schlosser (2001). Schlosser took on McDonalds,
Coca Cola and their sorts... without getting sued! Proof that documentation does not
have to get in the way of storytelling.


Mark Schapiro's book _Exposed: The Toxic Chemistry of Everyday Products and_
_What's at Stake for American Power (2009)_ is an example of his craftsmanship. I
think he’s the single best environmental investigative reporter around. He set the
standard for a generation after him (in which I count myself), showing that
environmental reporting is not for treehuggers or the fainthearted.


David Kaplan, GIJN executive director (US), recommends:


A selection of winners from the book category of the IRE Awards over the past 15
years. These are works chosen by fellow investigative journalists in large part for
their impressive methodology and intensive research. I’ve listed below books which
may have a more international appeal. You can see the full list going back to 1979
at: http://www.ire.org/awards/


2011: _Render Unto Rome_ : The Secret Life of Money in the Catholic Church, by
Jason Berry


2010: _Anatomy of An Epidemic_ : Magic Bullets, Psychiatric Drugs, and the
Astonishing Rise of Mental Illness in America, by Robert Whitaker

Eavesdropping on America, by James Bamford

Drogin

Wright

Men, by David Protess and Rob Warden.


6 1


Chapter 7

### **Finding** **open sources**


Reading:
Story-Based Inquiry, Chapter 4.
The Hidden Scenario (op. cit.)


Wadih Al Absi, “Yemen’s women inmates: disowned by family, cast out by
society, forced back into crime”. Arij.net, Nov. 12, 2013. PLEASE INSERT
LINK TO ARABIC


You may inform your students that this article will be used as the basis for a


**Introduction: What are open sources?**


It is a misunderstanding that journalism is about finding facts; instead, it is about
finding reliable _sources_ . Once you find the right documentary and human sources,
the facts will emerge from them. However, hunting for sources consumes the largest
share of a typical investigation. It is fair to say that 90% of journalists use only 10%
of the available sources, precisely because finding new sources takes time.


So careful planning is necessary. Generally, a plan should begin with open sources,
because they are the easiest to consult.


“Open sources” are people and documents that are freely and easily accessible. They
include:


People who seek public attention, and therefore willingly talk to
journalists. They may include: victims, lawyers, counselors, politicians,
competitors, campaigners, unionists, scientists, researchers, or
analysts (such as financial analysts).


Documents and data that are freely available to the public.


B. Putting documents first


If you ask your students how they can prove their hypotheses, nearly all of them will
reply: _Find someone who knows the answers to our questions._


For investigators, it is usually far more efficient and fruitful to seek information from
public documents first. The information they contain can confirm whether or not a
story is plausible, before much more time is invested in it.


You can expect that students will ask: “Why would we take the effort to obtain
documents? Why not stick to interviews? People are the best sources. Besides, the
information in public documents is already known!”


Possible answers include:


The fact that something is open to inspection does not mean that anyone
inspects it. Often, publicly available documents contain answers to
important questions, but no one has bothered to study the documents.
Story-Based Inquiry contains several examples of important stories that
were based on documents that no journalists bothered to consult, such
as statistics on premature birth, voting lists, scientific studies, etc.

Open sources, in particular documentary sources, can be consulted
without raising an alarm. Calling an official for an interview will attract
attention, and doors may close.


6 3


Documents often contain facts that interview subjects will not
spontaneously reveal.

Documents often save you the trouble of interviewing. Experts and other
sources have often expressed their experiences and opinions long before
you, as a journalist, think to interview them.


Even if someone is willing to speak with you, he or she will provide better
information if you are visibly capable of understanding it. The source
will observe whether or not you understand the language and concerns
of the people involved in your story. You must therefore do at least some
documentary research in order to prepare for interviews.


C. How to search for open sources


In order to search efficiently for documents and friendly human sources, we can use
the following method:


First we identify the people who are concerned by our story, and the
open sources that may be related to them.


Then we plan a route among these sources concerned by our story, and
the open sources that may be related to them. if we know whom we
expect or hope to meet, we have a better chance of being prepared when
an opportunity appears. If we have a strategy to reach them, our
chances of making contact increase.


1. Using a timeline to identify open sources


Wadih Al Absi’s story on women inmates of Yemeni prisons, the first investigation he
ever conducted, is based on a simple, tragic sequence of events:


Women fall into poverty.


They commit crimes in order to survive.


They are arrested by police.


The court sentences them to prison.


They serve their sentences.


When they come out of prison, they are abandoned by their families, and
return to crime.


We can imagine these other, following steps:


Scholars study the problem.


NGOs try to help them, but lack the means.


The government and parliament try to solve the problem.


6 4


In each of these events, someone accomplishes an action. Ask your students: _Which_
_of these characters will create a documentary record of his or her actions? Which of_
_them will use a document to justify his or her actions?_


The answers may include:


School or employment records that help to explain how a woman
became poor.


Criminal laws that justify arrests.


Arrest records.


Court documents, such as charges and judgments.


Prison records and procedures (for example, whether or not prisoners
receive vocational or religious instruction).


NGO reports or program descriptions.


Government or legislative reports.


This was Wadih Al Absi’s experience: Official sources would not speak with him. The
government did not regard these poor women as a problem worthy of notice. There
were thus no government reports, and no legislation. Nor were there academic
studies, or even press articles. NGOs were glad to see his interest, but could provide
very little information. Police and prison records were closed to him.


How, then, could the journalist advance?


Ask your students: _On the list of documents that actors in the story will create, which_
_are the ones that no one can stop us from obtaining?_


Wadih Al Absi’s answer, he said, was “to go back to the law, and look for gaps in the
law.” No one could stop him from reading Yemen’s laws. In the process, he made a
key discovery, which we extract from his story:


_Even within prison walls, the law for rehabilitation is rarely executed_


_When it comes to rehabilitation, inmates within prisons are no better off than_
_ex-prisoners outside, despite the fact that Yemen’s Prison Act that stresses the_
_importance of in-prison rehabilitation._
_Article 3 of the Prison Act states that “the treatment of prisoners should aim towards_
_their reformation and rehabilitation, through all relevant means and educational_
_processes, including training in services and in manual and social skills, as well as_
_physical, cultural and leisure activities.”_


6 5


This discovery was the lever that opened other doors to the reporter:


_But in reality, this law is far from the practice within correctional facilities. Prisons fail_
_to rehabilitate their inmates, emotionally and behavioural, experts say. According to_
_the president of the National Women’s Committee “rehabilitation programs are rare,_
_and when they are carried out, it is only in a few prisons.”_
_Members of parliament interviewed for this investigation agree. “A prisoner needs an_
_environment of care and preparation for life outside prison,” MP Shawqi Al Qadi says._
_Abdullah Al Hakim, another MP, acknowledged the insufficiency of governmental_
_support. “A survey is currently underway and has so far covered ten prisons in Yemen,_
_looking into fields such as reformation, rehabilitation, fostering and safety,” Al Hakim_
_said. “The results are not satisfying, and any type of care seems to be almost entirely_
_absent.”_


In fact, the government does not care about these women, as a statement from a
public speech reveals:


_At the latest national conference, however, the Minister of Social Affairs, Dr. Ama Al_
_Razzaq Hamad, stated that supporting the women’s shelter “is not among the_
_Ministry’s duties.”_


Throughout this process, said Wadih Al Absi, he was “building his knowledge.”
Eventually, high officials of Yemen’s prison system agreed to speak with him, along
with a wide range of civil society experts. He had amply demonstrated that he was
worth talking to, and could understand what he was told. This, too, is something
that we can obtain from public documents: Insight into the language and mindset of
the people whom we hope to interview.


C. Mapping a source village


The timeline shows us actors who are visibly involved in the story. But there may be
others who are affected by the story, or who are interested by the problems that it
raises. In the case above, they might include parents and friends of the unfortunate
women, confidants to whom they reveal their feelings, government officials who
regret the absence of a policy to help them, and others.


We can imagine whom these other characters may be by creating a “source village”.
Along with actors in the story, the “village” contains various witnesses to events or
their consequences. An example of a source village map, which indicates the
different actors involved in an unfolding tragedy centered on handicapped children,
can be found in Chapter 4 of Story-Based Inquiry. You will see that this is a very
simple technique.


Yet it is also powerful. Besides helping us to think of new sources, a source village
can help us to visualize the sources we already know, and their relations to each
other.


This is particularly useful when investigating business or criminal networks.
Complex chains of crossed ownership or alliances can be very difficult to define. By
situating the actors in space, we can have a far better idea of what we are dealing
with. It is no coincidence that the Organised Crime and Corruption Reporting Project
(OCCRP), a world leader in the field, has developed free software to create such

maps, which they call “Visual Investigation Scenarios.” _1_ (Scholars of network studies
have their own, expensive software to map complex interactions involving hundreds
of actors.)


_1_
We urge the instructor to visit _http://vis.occrp.org/_ for a direct experience of this technique and tool.


6 6


6 7


You can show your students how to map a source village:


In the middle of a sheet of paper or blackboard, students may write the
name of the principal actor in their story, and draw a circle around it.

Then, they may add other circles, containing the names of other actors
or categories of actors (such as “police”), arranged around the principal
actor.


Lines may be drawn to indicate relationships among the different actors.


The map should be sufficiently detailed to provide a clear image of the stakeholders
who are or may be involved, and to suggest different possibilities for finding human
sources. The instructor may note that journalists who focus on inside sources at the
start of an investigation leave themselves with few options in the event that their
targets refuse to speak. By identifying numerous opportunities to contact people, we
ensure that at least some information will flow to us.


The instructor may note: _In a village, everyone knows everyone else’s business. So_
_when you enter one house, very soon others in the village will know you are there. Be_
_sure you do not enter the village before you know something about the people who live_
_there. Otherwise, you will go to the wrong house, or leave the wrong impression._


Wadih Al Absi’s story can also be fruitfully used for this exercise, which can be
managed as a class discussion. Each source cited in this story represents a different
institution or organization that is involved with the central actors of the story – the
women whose bad luck becomes an unbreakable destiny. _2_


The instructor asks students to name the actors in the story, and to place them in
view on a blackboard or paperboard, like an aerial view of a village.


When the map is finished, the instructor may say: _We begin our interviews with the_
_people who are most open to us. Which house in this village will be most open to us?_
_Which will be most closed?_ In this story, organisations that help the women victims
offered the widest open doors.


However, the instructor may note that reporters must take great care not to become
the toy of organisations or individuals that offer access. Sources who control
information may decide to show only the parts that advance their interests. Thus
once the reporter has entered the village, and learned useful information, he or she
must proceed to others. You may ask the students: _Who is connected to our first_
_sources? These are the most likely prospects for our next round of interviews._


_2_
The story also illustrates that sources may turn out to be very different from what we might expect. The
director of the country’s prisons appears as one of the main advocates of reform and delivers a perfect


D. Take-home assignments


1. Cataloguing open sources


a.


b.


c.


d.


Before the next class session, students should create a source village map based
on their hypotheses and timelines. The maps should include not only characters
directly involved in actions, but those who are indirectly involved as witnesses.
The maps should include at least 10-15 direct or indirect sources.


Also before the next class session, your students should identify which events in
their timeline can be verified from open sources. Then, they may create a
detailed description of at least two open documentary sources that they intend
to use. The description may include:


The exact name of the source;


Its physical address (including the URL for its website) and contact
information if pertinent;


Its opening hours (for offices or institutions) and conditions of access
(reservations, fees, sign-on, etc.);


The information that can be accessed there, and why it is valuable for a
reporter;


A brief description of how best to work with the source (i.e., problems to
avoid, best practices). The description should enable anyone who wants
to use the source to do so effectively and efficiently.


Students should be informed that they will be graded on these descriptions,
according to whether the descriptions enable effective access to the open source.
The grade will take into account the eventual importance of the source for other
journalists. For example, if they discover a library where copies of all municipal
contracts are open to public inspection, that is a major source. So is the
procedure to be followed for obtaining property or business records.


Students should also be informed that their descriptions, if they are of
professional quality, will be added to a regional database of open sources. This
database, modeled on the open source listings first compiled by IRE, will be
available to journalists from the region and the world. Students’ names will
appear as authors for the descriptions they provide.


ARIJ has compiled basic listings for a number of countries in the Arab region, which
can be used as a model by your students. The listings appear as an annex to this
chapter.


6 8


2. Backgrounding a local company


a.


b.


c.


d.


Divide the students into teams of 2.


Each team is responsible for “backgrounding” a certain business firm, which the
instructor will select, or the students may propose. It is better if students
propose a firm in a sector that interests them personally. If a student rides
motorcycles, suggest that he or she background a firm that imports, repairs or
sells these vehicles, for example. Students should choose a sector or place that
interests them. The local café will do, or any business they know. However, they
can’t simply write a portrait of their cousins or parents who have a shop, a
cabinet or a corporation. They will have to obtain documentation.


If there is a Freedom of Information law in their country, they must study it, and
understand fully what rights it gives them. As a matter of best practice, they can
carry a copy of the law with them when they visit government offices.


Students will seek the following information from open sources, including
personal observation, government offices, the company website, the public
library, and news archives:


Who owns the firm;


Its address and the size of its offices or facilities;


Its annual results in recent years;


The number of employees, and whether the number is increasing or
decreasing;


Its physical assets (like property) and their visible condition (for
example, if it is a restaurant, is it clean? If the company owns trucks, do
they seem well-maintained? If it owns buildings, do they need repairs?);


Whether the firm or its owners have been noticed by news media or
social media, and if so, what was said.


6 9


3. Backgrounding a listed Company


The instructor will identify a listed company that does business in the region. The
company may be national, regional or foreign.

The instructor may ask students to obtain:


the latest annual report for the company;


any quarterly reports filed since the annual report;
