any public relations announcements that the company has issued in the
past year;


any news reports involving the company.


Students must then read the documents and write a brief report detailing any
information contained in these documents that is of current relevance in their own
country (for example, that the firm plans to sell certain foreign holdings). The report
can also answer some basic questions, such as: _How does the firm make money?_
_What has changed in the firm over the past three years? Is it gaining or losing_
_employees, or market share, or revenues?_


_Annex to Chapter Seven:_
### **Advanced** **Internet** **Search**


0 3


**Introduction**


Compared to most other investigative resources, the Internet appears relatively new
and still much in development. This makes it harder to give instruction on how to
use it. Websites that proved practical for researchers yesterday may have
disappeared today. Tricks that seem valuable today, may become obsolete tomorrow.

Nevertheless, some useful websites and tricks have proven sustainable. In this
chapter we suggest several that may be taught to students. In no way do we claim to
be complete or final. Instead, the goal is to demonstrate to students that their
Internet search can become far more efficient and powerful than it previously was.


The core strategy here is to put sources before information. Students can be
trained, instead of looking for an answer on the Internet, to look for reliable sources,
and then to look for the answer in these sources. The Internet is not a source. It is a
container of sources, like a library. As in a library, one first must decide which
source to pick before one starts reading.


Students often think that Google has all the answers, without realising that Google
shows answers from all kinds of sources, from pretty reliable to plain rubbish. To the
extent they decide what they want to see, they can eliminate a great deal of wasted
time and useless material. Students should be advised that “search costs” - the time
and effort it takes to find the solution to a given problem - are a main reason that
investigation is regarded by editor and publishers as slow and costly. By improving
their efficiency, they make investigative reporting a viable enterprise.


The most frequent problem that students encounter is that their search requests are
not precise enough. They should understand that Google’s default search mode is
Boolean - that is, Google will automatically search for documents containing all of
the terms in a request. To narrow the number of results, the easiest solution is thus
to repeat the request with additional terms. Many students are also unaware that by
putting terms between quotation marks, they direct Google to search for that exact
phrase. The difference is easily demonstrated: If students search for their full names
without quotation marks, everyone who shares either their forename and family
name and is indexed by Google will appear in the results.


Below, we have described common situations that journalists encounter in using the
Internet for research. Any or all of these situations may serve as the basis for
classroom exercises in which students use or add to the tools we provide below. We
have on occasion offered extra credit to students who discover useful new techniques
or websites. In any case, students should understand that their ability to use such
tools can make a great difference in their careers.


7 1


**I. We know exactly who can answer**
**a particular question**


A. Using an organisational website


Go to a website owned by the source or subject and see if you can find the answer
there. If you, for instance, want to know how much profit the Coca-Cola Company
makes from its soft drinks, you turn directly to their official website
(www.coca-colacompany.com) rather than search through thousands of results in
Google.


If a protagonist or antagonist in a story is a company, a NGO, an organisation or a
public figure, then he or she probably owns a website that publishes “official”
information.. Not all of the information will be reliable, of course, but there is
probably also stuff that is useful, like:


Organisational purpose (“mission statement”)


Financial reports


Organigram or structure of the organisation


Organisational history


Project or product information


News and press releases


Speeches and statements


Calendar of upcoming events


Links or alliances with other organisations

Names of possible sources with contact information


**II. We know what type of source can**
**answer a question**


A. Refining the search with “operators”


In formulating a search request for Google, an “operator” means a formula that
directs the search engine to focus only on particular locations and materials.
Operators are highly useful, but students will need to be shown how to use them; few
will make the effort for themselves. The examples below can serve as the basis for
classroom exercises.


7 2


1. The Minus sign (-)


The minus sign tells Google to exclude words from the search. Suppose we are
searching for health effects of Coca-Cola. To be fair to the firm, we start by searching
for: “Coca-Cola” is healthy. This brings us 1.35 million results, many sponsored by
the firm. Now let’s try it using a minus sign on “healthy”, and adding the term
“unhealthy”. This search is designed to cut out the firm’s promotion, and to highlight
critical opinions: _“Coca-Cola” is -healthy unhealthy._ That brings us 768,00 results,
from which the company’s promotion is largely absent.


2. The “site” operator


Google often finds more results in an official website than the embedded search bar
on that website. This is because of restrictions that webmasters often program into
their internal search engines. An external engine like Google, on the other hand, has
no such restrictions.


Thus, instead of using the search bar that is embedded on a website like
Coca-Cola’s, we can ask Google to look inside any website for key words. The syntax
is to give the search term, then the site where we will search for it. To do this we use
the “site:” operator. For example, if we use the search terms profit
site:coca-colacompany.com, Google will return pages on the Coca-Cola website that
contain the word “profit”.


We can also use the site: command to isolate websites from a particular domain.
Suppose we want to know what people in Jordan say about Coca-Cola. We search:
Coca-Cola site:jo This brings 1810 results, and they tell us a great deal about the
firm’s local operations.


The most useful domains include:


site:com returns only results from commercial websites


site:org from organisations


site:edu from universities (often but not exclusively American)


site:gov from the United States government


site:mil from the United States army


Note: Though the domains “gov” and “mil” are reserved for USA-based organisations,
their websites may contain useful general information, as well as facts about other
countries than the USA.


site:int from international treaty-based organisations like the United
Nations and Nato.


For example: _coca-cola site:int_ in Google returns stories about the firm on the
websites of USAID and the World Health Organisation (WHO).


7 3


Different nations may have different variants on the above domains. The “gov”
domain, for example, is written “fgov” in Belgium, “gouv” in France, “guv” in Romania
and “gov.uk” in the United Kingdom. Your students must check the domains that are
used by organisations they wish to explore with the site: operator.


3. The filetype: operator


Filetype: directs Google to search only for files in a particular format. This can be
highly useful when trying to track down a document that was probably created in a
given format.


Suppose, for example, that we wanted to see PowerPoint presentations about how
Coca-Cola markets its products. We could try this: Coca-Cola marketing filetype:ppt
There are over 17,000 such presentations indexed on Google, many of them created
by experts in the business or university sectors.


A key file type for reporters is .xls -- the format used for spreadsheets and databases.
(In Open Office, the extension is .sdc.) Let’s see if we can find any spreadsheets about
Coca-Cola marketing: Coca-Cola marketing filetype:xls It turns out that there are
80,000 available, pertinent to market share, share price, and other data. One of
these might save us some time that would otherwise be spent entering data into a
spreadsheet.


4. Other useful operators


allinurl: makes Google look for the keywords in the address of a webpage
(URL).


Example 1: If you write in Google: _allinurl:annual report site:coca-colacompany.com_
you will obtain pages dedicated to the official annual financial statements on
Coca-Cola’s website.


Example 2: Suppose we want to find webpages that claim Coca-Cola is unhealthy.
The search looks like this: Coca-Cola _allinurl:unhealthy_ We get 1760 results. Now
let’s try it the other way around, to see who holds a different view: Coca-Cola
_allinurl:healthy_ Here we get 55,000 results, many from the company itself. Does this
tell you whether or not the product is healthy or unhealthy? No. But it certainly tells
you who is thinking about the issue.


Most official websites offer a so-called “sitemap” – a kind of index on
their pages. By typing in Google:
allinurl:sitemap site:coca-colacompany.com you get a sitemap of
different sitemaps (!) with links to important Coca-Cola pages.


Journalists often want to consult the contact page of a website. Google
will get it quickly: allinurl:contact site:coca-colacompany.com


When a “404” message signals that a website does not exist anymore,
the operators cache: or info: followed by the url of that website may
score a hit in Google’s archives. An alternative strategy: Use the
“wayback machine” at www.archive.org, the Internet archive site.


7 4


7 5


**III. We want a finished document on a given subject**


Books and articles, as well as data, are kept in online libraries and databases; most
of these don’t allow search engines like Google to see their contents. If we want to
look inside, we need to go to the website containing the database and search there.

There are rich and reliable online databases in different categories. We advise that
students bookmark these sites on their computers: _1_


A. Secret documents


In recent years databases containing documents of special interest to investigative
reporters have been created.


Some of these, like Wikileaks (http://wikileaks.org) are of demonstrated
interest to the Arab world; the exposure of American diplomatic disdain
for Ben Ali was among the factors that drove the Tunisian revolution.The
archive still exists, and currently contains about 800,000 documents.


Before Wikileaks existed, there was Cryptome: http://cryptome.org. Its
archive contains over 71,000 documents at this writing.


Currently, the Document Cloud is showing how investigative reporters
can contribute materal to a common archive
(http://www.documentcloud.org/home).


As an exercise, you can direct students to search any of these databases for material
of local interest.


B. Laws and court rulings


An increasing number of governments publish their laws and judicial rulings on
websites. Even foreign laws can have local impact (for example, strict European
Union environmental regulation has led to dumping of toxic products in the
developing world). ARIJ is compiling such sources for the Arab World.


For the European bureaucracy, useful platforms include:


http://www.eu4journalists.eu


http://www.searcheuropa.eu


_1 These links, and an Internet Toolbox, can aso be found at: www.storybasedinquiry.com/search_


C. Scholarly Research


Journalists have a dreadful habit of relying on secondary sources for scholarly and
scientific articles - that is, a newspaper story about a discovery - instead of the
primary source. Investigators need the primary sources, and they are not hard to
find in specialised databases and search engines:


http://scholar.google.com (a familiar interface and deep content of
scholarly references in every field; however, limited access to the
documents);


http://www.sciencedirect.com (very complete, and includes significant
open access resources);


http://www.faganfinder.com/science (a meta-search engine that allows
you to explore several scientific databases);


http://www.dart-europe.eu (European doctoral theses; currently being
updated)


D. Books


Online libraries do not replace physical libraries as an archival source; nonetheless,
they may contain great resources. You may wish to verify if your national library
offers an online catalogue. International online libraries include:


http://books.google.com (allows you to preview or, in many cases, read
books for free);


http://www.worldcat.org (informs you of the physical location of books);


http://www.loc.gov/index.html (site of the US Library of Congress,
containing vast amounts of open information of international interest;
the catalogue returned 260 hits for the subject, “Tunisia corruption”)


E. Company data


Students may bookmark national or regional websites of company registers, where
one finds financial and social information about companies and non-profits as well.
A very powerful source of international company data, with links to numerous
national sites, is found at: http://www.investigativedashboard.org


7 6


F. Encyclopedias


We all know that cutting and pasting from Wikipedia has hardly advanced student
skills, knowledge or ethics. Nonetheless, online encyclopedias offer a valuable
introduction to many subjects, subject to verification by other sources. Wikipedia
offers an international list of encyclopedias, many offering free access:
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_online_encyclopedias


Though not quite an encyclopedia,,the US Central Intelligence Agency’s “World
Factbook” deserves special mention, since it contains extensive information on
virtually every country:
https://www.cia.gov/library/publications/the-world-factbook/index.html


**IV. We have no clue who can answer a question**


In some Arab countries social media comprise the main independent news channels,
as well as the best way to reach individuals.


Twitter can be searched as well as followed. The service offers its own
search engine: https://twitter.com/search-advanced


Besides an archive of tweets to 2006, http://topsy.com provides
analytics and trends, including free basic services.


For most students, Facebook search can be greatly improved, on
condition that the students has a Facebook account. The procedure is to
go to settings and switch the language to “English US”.


This opens up one of the most powerful search engines at the moment: Facebook
Graph. The student may then type a request in the search bar on his or her
Facebook page. For example: _Females who live in Amman, Jordan and worked for_
_Coca-Cola_ offers dozens of local female contacts who can talk about the Coca-Cola
company.


**V. We want to know who owns a website**


It is possible to check who registered any given website. Both http://whois.com and
http://www.domaintools.com will show details like name, address (and in the latter
case, phone number) for the person who registered a domain name. That may not be
the person we are looking for, however; one can also hit a registration company that
has no connection to the content of the website. Nonetheless, we have used both
these sites to verify ownership of websites with litigious contents - an important
guarantee that we are speaking of the right person when we discuss the responsible
party.


7 7


Chapter 8

### **A brief and selective** **history of investigative** **journalism before** **Watergate**

**/ 1.5 hours**


A. Overview


Investigative journalism has evolved in cycles, and there are two primary drivers of
these movements. Either is sufficient to sustain the genre. When both appear
simultaneously, there is explosive development of investigative content, formats and
audiences.


1.


2.


One driver resides in the development and exploitation of new reporting
methods. Sometimes these methods follow the appearance of new sources of
information that can be readily accessed. They may also result from the
reporter’s willingness to adopt a different role or approach to the work.


A second key force resides in the willingness of a significant public to hear what
investigators say, and to support their calls for change. Such publics are not an
unchanging, faceless mass. They may include specific classes, sectors or
communities within society, or particular groups within ruling elites (as is often
the case in China). The public’s attention is never guaranteed, and it is never
permanent. Journalists and their allies must continually seek to attract the
interest and backing – financial, social and ethical – of influential audiences. A
key factor here is the ability of journalists to find novel ways of telling stories.
Another is their ability to form alliances with other social actors.


Below, we offer a summary history of investigative reporting, focused mainly (but not
entirely) on the United States.  The instructor should be clear with students that the _1_
US is neither a unique nor ideal model of how investigative journalism can develop.
The genre has been practiced in one form or another in numerous countries since
the 19th century. Prisons were often a focus of early investigative work. Australian
editor Henry Melville spent time in prison for exposing harsh prison conditions in the
1830s;  in France, Albert Londres’s 1923 exposé of the prison colony of Cayenne, _2_ _Au_
_Bagne_, remains a classic; in South Africa, investigations of prison conditions were
published by Drum magazine in the 1950s. Following the Second World War, there
was a worldwide surge of protesting, critical journalism in countries that enjoyed
some degree of press freedom, with another wave beginning in the 1960s. _3_


_1_


_2_


_3_


7 9


However, the US was the first country to institutionalize the practice of investigative
reporting simultaneously in universities, civil society groups and the news industry.
Because this movement was deep and wide, it also allows us to compare events and
actors in the US with similar elements elsewhere. Some students, and even some
professors, will find this fact offensive; once again, they will say, the Americans are
claiming credit for everything that is good! In fact, there is nothing more offensive in
the historical fact that Americans were innovators for investigative reporting, than
there is in the historical fact that Arabs largely invented modern mathematics.


Later, we will consider the emergence of investigative reporting as a self-consciously
global phenomenon, early in the 21st century. This leads to recent developments in
the Arab world.


_1 A different version of this material was first published in Mark Hunter, Le Journalisme d’investigation en France_
_et aux Etats-Unis. Paris: Presses universitaires de France, coll. Que sais-je?, 1997._


_2 Carson, A., The History of Investigative Reporting in Australia. Oxford University Press. An informative sample_
_chapter is available: http://lib.oup.com.au/he/media_journalism/samples/tanner_journalismresearch_sample.pdf_


_3 See McKnight, D., ‘The Investigative Tradition in Australian Journalism 1945–1965’, in_
_A. Curthoys & J. Schultz, eds, Journalism: Print, Politics and Popular Culture. University of Queensland Press, 1999._


B. “Colliding traditions”: Partisanship, Objectivity, and Crusading


The origins of the English-language newspaper were commercial, in the form of
letters offering confidential information to business interests, and partisan, in the
form of propaganda sheets for Puritan revolutionaries. Business and political
factions remained key publics for the news media.


a. Journalism as a moral force


In the early 19th century, some US and English newspapers sought to enlarge their
publics beyond partisan groups by focusing on crime and scandals. They claimed
that they presented an “objective” image of society, while protecting its interests from
wrongdoers. Later in the century “crusades” against corruption, or to repair the _4_
effects of natural disasters, or to promote causes like the Spanish-American War,
became prominent features of the Anglo-American press. We understand that the
term “crusades” has unpleasant meaning for Arab students; yet that was the term
employed at the time, and it was indeed deeply tinged with Christian moralism.
Journalists became guardians of public morality, exposing vice to promote virtue.


Two figures of this era are particularly noteworthy for our class. Both achieved fame
while claiming to use it in the service of reform. Both also innovated in terms of
journalistic technique. However, their innovations included deceptive methods, in
which the reporter pretended to be someone else. We will discuss undercover
reporting in more detail later in the term. At this point, you may note that a major
reason why reporters used undercover methods at this time was that other methods,
such as document searches, would not be developed until a generation later.


1.


Nellie Bly, a reporter for the New York World in the
1870s, can be considered the avatar of undercover
reporting. Her first great success, a series of articles
called “Ten Days in a Mad-House”, came about by _5_
pretending to be insane, and having herself interned
in a mental hospital, which she described as “a tomb
of living horrors.” She later played the roles of a
corrupt lobbyist, an immigrant and a maid for her
readers. To this day, undercover reporting remains
highly popular with the public and leading media.
The German author Gunther Wallraff has built an
international reputation by playing undercover
roles, including a Turkish immigrant and an
employee of a tabloid newspaper. Contemporary
television reporters, such as the 101 East team of
Al-Jazeera, rely heavily on undercover techniques to
obtain confidences from witnesses.


_4 Dan Schiller, "An Historical Approach to Objectivity and Professionalism in American News Reporting." Journal of_
_Communication 29:4, pp. 46-57 (1979)._


_5 The articles can be read for free at: http://digital.library.upenn.edu/women/bly/madhouse/madhouse.html!_


8 0


2.


At London in the 1880s, William T. Stead of the Pall
Mall Gazette created a sensation by reporting how he
procured a virgin for allegedly sexual purposes, to
create outrage at child prostitution. Stead employed
a former prostitute and brothel keeper to assist his
project, published as a sensational four-part series,
“The Maiden Tribute of Modern Bablyon”. He _6_
organized a “Special and Secret Committee of
Inquiry,” including a prominent feminist
campaigner, to support and widen the project – an
early and prophetic example of how reporters may
use coalitions of social forces to defend their work.
He was also one of the first journalists to use
extended interviews as an investigative tool.
Interviews that last longer than an hour require
great stamina and preparation on the part of the
reporter. They can also lead to far more detailed and
accurate revelations from sources.


William Stead


8 1


b. The emergence of document-based advocacy reporting


At the end of the 19th century a new trend became visible simultaneously in the US
and France: the use of government documents to support investigative journalism.
The trend emerged as reporters sought methods that would offer them an advantage
over powerful or wealthy adversaries. In France, the adversary was the State, which
had falsely accused an army officer named Dreyfus of espionage. Emile Zola’s
famous essay about the Dreyfus Affair, _J’Accuse_, was based largely on a long
investigation by reporter Bernard Lazare, _L'Affaire Dreyfus – Une erreur judiciaire_
(1896). Lazare’s core method was to verify the prosecution’s own statements, thus
demonstrating their weakness and errors. Similarly, in the US, Ida Tarbell’s
ground-breaking investigation of the Standard Oil monopoly  was based principally _7_
on public documents – in particular, transcripts of testimony from trials involving
the firm.


_6 These articles are available free at_
_http://dlib.nyu.edu/undercover/maiden-tribute-modern-babylon-w-t-stead-pall-mall-gazette_

_6 The History of the Standard Oil Company, 2 vols,1904._


Lazare was an isolated figure: The overwhelming
majority of Parisian newspapers rejected, ignored or
attacked his work. Investigative reporting failed to
become an entrenched feature of French media until
late in the 20th century. The key point here is that
anyone who wants a career as an investigative
reporter needs allies. If he or she cannot find them at
home, they must look abroad. We will say more
about this when we discuss the global investigative
journalism movement. In contrast, Ida Tarbell was
part of a movement, at once social and professional,
that addressed a wide range of subjects.


Collier’s or Cosmopolitan,  which published reporters like Tarbell and her colleagues _8_
Lincoln Steffens and Ray Stannard. (Today, investigative blogs often play the same
role as those weekly magazines.) The reporters were denounced by President
Theodore Roosevelt as “the [people] with a muck-rake,” who could not stop poking
and handling the filth in life. Thus they got their nickname, “muckrakers”, which is
still used for investigative reporters. Then as now, journalists who denounce the
mistakes or crimes of the powerful are accused of undermining society, rather than
reforming it. Another tactic that is still used against investigative reporters emerged
in reaction to the muckrakers: Business interests that were challenged by their work
retaliated by cancelling advertising contracts with the weekly magazines.


Yet the muckrakers obtained major reforms in
American democracy. Thanks to Tarbell, the
Sherman Antitrust Act, enacted by the US Congress
in 1890, was finally employed in 1906 against the
most fearsome of monopolies, the Standard Oil
Company. Upton Sinclair opened another front in
1906 with _The Jungle_, a novel that documented the
horrific filth and violence of the Chicago meat
industry. Note here that certain kinds of fiction can
be highly effective tools for investigators and others
seeking to change society. In Egypt, Naguib Mahfouz
used the novel form to critique and expose social
conditions,


The Jungle led to the immediate passage of the Pure Food and Drug Act, landmark
regulatory legislation. In 1919, Sinclair attacked the corruption of the popular press
in _The Brass Check_, which he published himself. It resulted in a new code of ethics,
including the demand that journalists be “objective” and neutral toward the facts of
a story, by the Society of Professional Journalists.


This was only the first time that the expansion of investigative journalism led to
intense interest in the ethics of the profession. The same phenomenon occurred after
the Watergate Affair in the US (1976), and after the Contaminated Blood Affair in
France (1991). It has been underway in Tunisia since the fall of Ben Ali. It can safely
be said that it will occur anywhere that journalists acquire new powers through their
own initiative. The implication is that your students must be certain of the ethical
validity of their work and their methods.


_8 The instructor may wish to note thet Cosmoplitan is no longer a champion of investigative journalism._

_9 Robert_ _D_ _Reynolds Jr_ _"The 1906 Campaign to Sway Muckraking Periodicals " Journalism Quarterly_


8 2


Upton Sinclair


The Progressive Era is generally considered as
having ended with the election of Woodrow Wilson,
who promised stability as well as reform, to the
Presidency in 1912. The Muckrakers had enjoyed
one decade at the forefront of public attention. One
can imagine that the public needed a rest from
revelations of corruption and danger in every sphere
of society; something similar would take place in the
decade after Watergate in the US, and in France after
the Contaminated Blood Affair in 1992. In all these
cases, period of invention and expansion in
investigative reporting were succeeded by lulls.
Typically, one would then hear that “investigative
reporting is dead.” More exactly, both reporters and
the public were catching their breaths before the
next wave.


8 3


The instructor can note that investigative reporting is not always the public’s
favorite genre. However, it never goes away, and sooner or later, it will again be
in high demand. A young journalist is wise to acquire these skills, because they
contribute directly to a long and successful career.


In all of these periods, the use of exciting new reporting methods – such as
undercover work, extended interviewing, challenge to official “truths” based on
ample documentation – created a wide public for investigative revelations.
Eventually, investigative journalists challenged a broad range of institutions and
personalities. Through a variety of methods, they fought back. Moreover, public
authorities offered at least some reforms that calmed the public’s anger. Eventually,
in many different areas and countries, investigative reporting became an
institutionalised practice aimed at ongoing reform.


c. The corruption of investigative reporting


The history of investigative reporting includes some dark chapters, and your
students must be aware of them. The possession of exclusive or secret information
confers power, and that power can be used for criminal gain as well as the public
good.


France offers many examples. Around the time Bernard Lazare was investigating the
Dreyfus Affair, the newspaper _Le Matin_ undertook a famous campaign of blackmail
against the King of Belgium. Between the world wars another Parisian daily, _Le_
_Quotidien_, was paid by the Bank of France to attack the Minister of Finances, as well
as to suppress articles critical of the Bank. It is worth saying that when investigative _10_
reporting re-emerged in France in the 1980s, the French had not forgotten such
scandals; they believed that journalists in general, and investigators in particular,
were probably acting on behalf of occult interests.


_10 Claude Bellanger et al., Histoire générale de la presse Francaise, Tome III. Paris:_
_Presses Universitaire de France, 1972._

_11 Mark Hunter, Ethical Conflict and Investigative Reporting: Le Monde and the Contaminated Blood Affair._


Following the collapse of the Soviet Union, such practices reappeared in numerous
countries of Eastern Europe. The privatisation of state assets created a new class of
“oligarchs”, whose wealth was acquired by buying up former public enterprises, and
maintained through alliances with political factions, intelligence services, police,
private militias or criminal gangs. Privatisation enabled them to buy media assets
cheaply. This led to “berlusconisation” of the media, including “private monopolies in
the broadcasting industry, strong overt and stealthy collusion of business interests
and media ownership [and] blatant partisanship in the media”. The media covered _12_
up scandals or criminal charges against their owners, attacked opponents or
competitors, and protected political allies. Independent journalists documented that _13_
in Rumania, reporters accepted payment from business interests in exchange for not
investigating them. _14_


_12_


_13_


_14_


Besides the corruption of money, investigative reporters can be corrupted by
power. The American columnist Walter Winchell used his immense popularity to
defend President Franklin D. Roosevelt, a hero of the common people impoverished
by the Depression. Winchell, a pioneer of gossip and celebrity reporting, sometimes
attacked critics of Roosevelt by exposing their private scandals. A few years after
Roosevelt’s death, Winchell joined the ignoble McCarthyite witch-hunt against
Communists. _15_


The point your students need to understand is that their sources and their
public will not necessarily assume that they are different from or better than
other journalists, simply because they undertake investigative reports. In
particular, they must not think that if sources do not trust them, it is a
personal insult; your students will draw this conclusion when sources lie to
them, as some sources will. They must prove that they are different, in their
way of working and in what they publish. You can begin a discussion of this
topic with your students by asking: “Why are people afraid of reporters? Are
they sometimes right to be afraid?”


d. The renewal of investigative reporting in the US before Watergate:
Models for students


i. “Inside” reporting


Investigative reporting did not vanish in the US with the Progressive Era. However, _16_
with some exceptions, the document-based reporting of the Muckrakers became less
evident and influential. Some of its place was taken by “inside” reporting – typically,
newspaper columnists who enjoyed access to Washington insiders, and who passed
on “leaks” and “tips” from them. This model, of journalists whose key asset is
exclusive access to prominent newsmakers, can be found wherever there are news
media.


_12 Dušan Relji_ ć _, “Civil Society, Mass Media and Democracy in Post-Communist Countries.” Stiftung Wissenschaft_
_und Politik: Deutsches Institut für Internationale Politik und Sicherheit (www.swp-berlin.org), Berlin, 2004, p. 14._

_13 Mark Lee Hunter and Luk N. Van Wassenhove, “KP Media (A): Building a media empire in Ukiraine.”_
_INSEAD case, 2012._

_14 See Sorin Ozon, “The Media Dinosaurs,” Romanian Centre for Investigative Journalism, 6 September 2005, via_
_https://www.crji.org/articles.php?id=4072&hi=dinosaur_


_15 The definitivbe study is Gabler, Neil, Winchell: Gossip, Power and the Culture of Celebrity. Vintage, 1995._


8 4


8 5


We urge the instructor to find a local example of such a journalist to discuss in class
with students. The key question is: Does this journalist discover information for him
or herself, or simply offer a platform to newsmakers?

Such icons may deliver or create scoops – for example, when American TV news
reporter Barbara Walters broadcast Egyptian President Anwar Sadat’s offer to go to
Jerusalem to make peace with Israel in the 1970s. However, their focus on scoops is
rarely compatible with investigation, because if they challenged their sources too
strongly, they would lose access. In consequence, frequently their “scoops” are
nothing more than what their sources want the public to hear.


In contrast, the journalist I.F. Stone, who was blacklisted from the newspaper
industry after exposing racism and pro-Fascist elements at the powerful Federal
Bureau of Investigation (FBI) in the 1940s, created a self-published newsletter, _I.F._
_Stone’s Weekly_ . It became one of the most influential publications in the US, despite
its small circulation. Though Stone also relied on tips from Washington insiders, his
key sources were public documents, including speeches, minutes of committee
hearings, the _Congressional Record_ and other government publications, which he
studied closely for signs of conflict or corruption.  He also innovated by showing that _17_
a reporter could gain influence by creating an alternative audience outside the news
industry.


Your students will believe that scoops are
synonymous with investigation. More
exactly, a real investigation is always a scoop,
because it reveals something that was
previously hidden or ignored. But not every
scoop is an investigation.


I.F. Stone


A good way to make this point is to use the ARIJ-funded story, “Hussein Salem
Completed one of the deals of selling ‘MEDOR’ on the Stock Exchange on a Friday
when it was closed”.” The authors refer to a scoop by a columnist in an Egyptian _18_
newspaper, who realized that something wrong had happened. Though the
columnist saw the story first, the investigation shows the mechanisms of corruption
in far greater detail.


The instructor should ask students: Is it enough to say that something
occurred, or is it also important to show how it occurred? Why is this
important? What difference does it make?


One answer is that the investigative story enables prosecution of the wrong-doer and
reform to make such crimes more difficult. The columnist was unable to achieve
that.


_17 An anthology edited by Karl Weber, The Best of of I.F. Stone, is available online as a free PDF file at_
_http://www.ifstone.org/collectedifstone.pdf. This website also includes a great deal of free documentation_
_on Stone, including a film ._


_18 Abdulrahman Shalabi and Ali Zalat for Al Masry Al Youm (Cairo), November 24th, 2011._
_Available_ _via ahttp://www arij net_


The instructor can also ask: Why are facts important? What do they change?


The answer here is that honest people can differ in matters of opinion. But they
cannot differ so easily, or so honestly, about demonstrated facts. It is true that many
“facts” are merely lies, created through false demonstrations. But this proves the
point that facts, in the end, are more powerful than opinions. Why else would liars
seek to “prove” their lies? It is also true that many viewers are indifferent to facts,
and are thus easily confused by professional liars and demagogues. However, the
minority of individuals and forces who most influence society are very concerned
with reality, because if they base their actions on illusions, they are far more likely
to fail. Your students should understand that in many cases their audience will be
an elite – the elite that cares about knowing the reality of their lives and their society.


ii. The revival of muckraking


a. Whistleblower stories


The 1960s saw the revival of whistleblower stories, in which an insider tells what he
saw, heard or discovered while working for an organization. The insider is not
necessarily a reporter, but his or her revelations have the power of a scoop. That was
the case with _Death at an Early Age_ (1968), a teacher’s first-hand account of racism _19_
in the Boston Public Schools, which forced reform in that system. which forced
reform in that system. Like an undercover reporter, the whistleblower does not
announce his or her purpose while inside the organization. However, the
whistleblower may have greater access to internal documents or insights than the
undercover reporter, who is limited in an investigation to what he or she can see
without revealing one’s true purpose or identity.


The success of _60 Minutes_, the CBS news show that was launched in 1968 and
became the greatest profit-maker of any show in US broadcasting history, was based
to a great extent on whistleblowers. The film _The Insider_ recounts the true story of a
whistleblower in the US tobacco industry, Jeffrey Wigand, who cooperated with _60_
_Minutes_ . Though fictionalized, the story accurately depicts the rewards and
difficulties of working with such sources. The instructor may consider making the
film available to students for class discussion. _20_


However, the instructor must insist upon a point that is not always fully appreciated
by viewers of this Hollywood drama: When whistleblowers work with reporters, the
whistleblowers often get hurt, while the reporters become famous. In many cases,
the whistleblower is eventually abandoned or even betrayed by the same media to
whom he or she gave the story. Ultimately, the reporter moves on to another story,
and the whistleblower must get on with what is left of life.


The instructor may ask: What can justify the betrayal of his or her employer by
a whistleblower? What do reporters owe to whistleblowers, beyond making use
of their information? How much control should the source have over the story?


_19 Kozol, Jonathan. Death at an Early Age: The Destruction of the Hearts and Minds of Negro Children in the Boston_
_Public Schools. (1967)._


_20 Michael Mann, director, The Insider, 1999._


8 6


Increasingly, smart whistleblowers insist on guarantees before they agree to
collaborate. To take a striking recent example, when whistleblowers in the UK
approached the news media to sell them a database showing all the expense reports
of UK parliamentarians, they demanded enough money to pay for their retirements
if they were caught (100,000 GBP), and a firm promise that all the material would be
published. The Daily Telegraph agreed. During 30 days, the newspaper published
the entire database in its pages, rewritten to emphasize dramatic discoveries. It was
a profitable investment: The Telegraph sold a cumulative 1 million copies more than
its usual sales while the series ran. _21_


For media, the question is not whether, but how that value can be captured,
and for whom. We consider value creation and capture more fully in Chapter 13.


b. Social turmoil and investigative style


In the US and Europe, the 1960s were a time of social upheaval, protest and
mobilization – not unlike what happened in the Arab Spring. Such turmoil always
represents an opportunity for innovation in the media. Just as Tunisian
revolutionaries turned Facebook from a social media into a news media in 2010-11,
muckrakers in the 1960s created new media or remade existing media into vehicles
for their work. There is no divine law which specifies that investigators can only use
one form of media to distribute their stories.


In the US, the key innovations of the 1960s involved content, style and distribution.
One channel that combined many such innovations was the “alternative” press –
mainly free weekly newspapers, typically published in cities with large student
populations, and supported by advertisers whose customers were those same
students. Although often lacking in professionalism, these weeklies covered cultural,
political and social issues that mainstream media ignored or misinterpreted, and
their success was lasting. Muckraking articles were among their key features. A
famous example is The Great Speckled Bird, an “underground” newspaper from
Atlanta, Georgie, USA, whose archives can be consulted free online.  The magazine _22_
Rolling Stone may be considered a variant of this formula. In the 1990s, free _23_
English-language weeklies became vital media in the former Soviet republics and
client states of Eastern Europe.  In other words, the “alternative weekly”, distributed _24_
free and supported by advertising revenue, has proven to be one of the most
successful, diverse and sustainable (though rarely wealthy) formats in the history of
investigative news media.


_22_


_23_


_24_


8 7


The instructor can also refer to the magazines, such as New York, New West (both
developed by the brilliant editor Clay Felker) and Esquire, where the “New
Journalism” flourished in the 1960s. The instructor’s key reading here will be Tom
Wolfe’s landmark anthology, _The New Journalism_ . The instructor may also wish to
assign one or another articles from the anthology for students.


_21 For a cogent account of how the Telegraph sustained the story, see its day-by-day summary :_
_http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/newstopics/mps-expenses/5324582/How-the-Telegraph-investigation-exposed-_
_the-MPs-expenses-scandal-day-by-day.html_


_22 See http://library.gsu.edu/gsb/_


_23 See Peck, Abe. Uncovering the sixties: The life and times of the underground press. New York: Pantheon books,_
_1985; McMillian, John. Smoking typewriters: The sixties underground press and the rise of alternative media_
_in America. Oxford University Press, 2009 ; Fountain, Nigel. Underground: the London alternative press, 1966-74._
_London: Routledge, 1988; Mathes, Rainer, and Barbara Pfetsch. "The role of the alternative press in the agenda-building_
_process: Spill-over effects and media opinion leadership."European Journal of Communication 6.1 (1991): 33-62._


The instructor must be clear that no article in this anthology can be called
investigative.


However, the book has inspired many investigative reporters. Its great contribution _25_
is to show that journalism can be made stronger, and even sometimes more credible,
by incorporating narrative devices from fiction, _on the condition that these techniques_
_do not replace, falsify or obscure the facts._ In particular:


The New Journalists used cinema-like scenes (instead of exposition, or
summaries of events) as the basic unit of their narratives. This enabled their
readers to experience the stories, as opposed to simply absorbing information.


Moreover, they treated their sources as characters, not as official or
institutional representatives. They thus showed the influence of personality
on events.


Most controversially, the New Journalists were not merely invisible observers
of the events described in their stories. Often, they were actors in the stories.
This allowed them to use their own reactions or insights as source material.


The instructor can note that all of these innovations carry risks. A reporter who gets
caught up in particular scenes may miss the larger flow of events. Focusing on
personality can distract a reporter from an individual’s acts and their consequences.
Worst of all, a reporter who takes front stage may signal that he or she is more
important than anyone or anything else in the story, which robs it of meaning.

Examples from the 1960s of how great writing can accompany investigative depth
are found in Jessica Mitford’s anthology, _Poison Penmanship_ (1979). Mitford’s work
combines hard-edged investigation with impudent, biting style. In particular, Mitford
showed how citing extended dialogues in scenes with sources, instead of providing
short quotes, could add both color and depth to a report. For example, she shows a
leading New York intellectual who says that he cheats students of his “Famous
Writers School” because they are stupid, and then smiles and tries to persuade her
not to quote him. The statement becomes even uglier when he tries to suppress it.


This technique directly influenced Carl Bernstein, who used it in his account of the
Watergate scandal. _27_


The key teaching points that can be drawn from these works are the following:


1.


2.


3.


Style is information. The way in which facts are delivered powerfully
influences their ultimate effect on the viewer.


Dialogue is the most powerful form of quoting sources. Dialogue carries not
only the source’s information, but the way and circumstances in which it is
expressed.


Reporters need to gather not only facts, but also details of character, setting,
and circumstances. These descriptive elements can reveal things that sources
do not say. They also help to build compelling narratives.


_25 For example, the great investigative sports reporter Andrew Jennings cites The New Journalism as his model of_
_style. See Hunter, Mark Lee, editor, The Global Investigative Journalism Casebook, UNESCO 2012, p. TK_

_26 This risk is analysed in detail in Hunter, M.L. and Sengers, L., “The Ethics of Investigative Style”, in The Story_
_Tells the Facts: Structuring and Composing Investigative Narratives. London: Logan/CIJ, 2012._


8 8


8 9


c. The revival of consumer watchdog reporting


It is a great error to believe that investigative reporting is the sole property and
heritage of the news media. Recall that _The Jungle_ (1906), a novel, was among the
most influential works of the Muckraker era. Likewise, one of the most powerful
investigations of the years before Watergate was written by an independent activist,
and published as a book. In 1965, Ralph Nader showed in _Unsafe at_ _Any Speed_ that
US car manufacturers sold vehicles that they knew contained deadly design flaws.
Nader linked those flaws to thousands of deaths, and warned: “The roots of the
unsafe vehicle problem are so entrenched that the situation can be improved only by
the forging of new instruments of citizen action.” The importance of car _28_
manufacturers as advertisers was surely one reason that until Nader appeared, news
media had failed to expose the story.


Nader became a popular speaker at universities, a novel revenue source for
investigative reporting. He also founded an association of consumers to fight for
reforms, supported by public donations. Eventually, he became a candidate for US
President, and though he lost, he also attracted millions of voters. His success
greatly influenced the news industry, where investigative reporting swiftly became a
feature of consumer columns.


Currently NGOs or civil society organisations aimed at consumer protection, as well
as commercial enterprises, publish magazines or websites focused on protecting
consumers from fraud or dangerous products. In nearly any market, there is room
for such a publication, or even several of them. The instructor may refer to examples
of such media, in particular:


_Consumer reports_ (http://www.consumerreports.org/cro/index.htm). This
American journal guides the purchase decisions of millions of people. Its
independent, rigorous product reviews can create great trouble for
manufacturers of faulty goods.


_60 Millions de Consommateurs_ (http://www.60millions-mag.com/). Backed
by a powerful civil society association, this group does not hesitate to confront
government as well as business.


_Prescrire (_ http://english.prescrire.org/en/). This online and print publication,
launched in French and now in English, offers a new variant on the classic
consumer journalism formula. It is focused on a professional audience,
medical doctors, who need an independent viewpoint on new treatments and
medications to counterbalance the aggressive promotional tactics of
pharmaceutical companies. Prescrire has exposed genuine scandals involving
unsafe medications.


_28 Excerpted at http://www.autolife.umd.umich.edu/Design/Gartman/Books/BK_Unsafe_Any_Speed.htm. The_
_original is: Ralph Nader, Unsafe at Any Speed The Designed-In Dangers of The American Automobile (1965)_
_Grossman_ _Publishers_ _New York_


In this as in other ways, investigative reporting has always been a force for
innovation. It has rarely been the mainstream of the news industry. However,
whenever investigative reporting advances, the news industry is forced to respond,
and to incorporate key features of the movement. This has two important
implications for your students:


1.


2.


3.


There are many paths through which your students can publish their work,
find an audience, and make a living, if they can offer verified, exclusive and
useful information. Especially in developing countries, the news industry is
often timid, or under the control of oligarchs. The fact that the news industry
is not ready for a given investigation, or a new way of presenting it, does not
mean that an ambitious reporter has no options.


There are many ways in which investigations can be presented to the public,
or to a specific audience. The only rule that cannot be broken is that the story
must be based on verified facts, and those facts must be as complete as
possible.

Investigative reporting is not only a way to influence politics. It can be used in
every domain of social and economic life as well.


9 0


_Annex to Chapter 8:_
### **The Emergence** **of Arab** **Investigative** **Reporting**


By Dr. Saba Bebawi


In the following essay, Saba Bebawi undertakes the first comprehensive
overview of the historical development of Investigative Journalism in
the Arab region. The instructor can use this unique material
in at least two ways:


First, this authoritative essay answers the question, “What about Arab
investigative journalism?” It provides a balanced and documented
assessment of the achievements of Arab journalists as they sought to
extend the boundaries of their craft under often difficult conditions.


Second, the text provides a foundation for further research, by the
instructor as well as the students. In particular, the instructor can ask
students to prepare a presentation on one of the journalists mentioned
in Bebawi’s text, following the guidelines in Chapter 16, “Finding
models: Class discussion of reading assignments and guest lecturers.”


When going through the historic development of Arab journalism, two observations
emerge:


The first observation is that the tradition of journalism that historically
evolved in the region, developed as a result of individual journalists
playing an active role in transforming the field. Today, investigative
journalists once again find themselves needing to transform the field
and becoming independent drivers of change.


The second point that emerges is that although Arab journalism evolved
based on Western traditions of journalism by borrowing many of their
values, it eventually transformed to become a form of journalism that
was specific to the region formed by political, social, economic and
cultural influences.


Today, Arab investigative journalism is being shaped in a similar way in the region:
by becoming a form of investigative reporting that is adopting Western teachings and
techniques of investigative reporting, whilst accommodating cultural practices
specific to the Arab culture of journalism. With these two observations in mind, I will
provide an historical account of how Arab journalism evolved in general, and
investigative journalism specifically, in the region.


9 2


In _The Press in the Arab Middle East: A History_, Ami Ayalon provides a
comprehensive and detailed account of the rise of journalism in the Arab world,
which I will rely on here as a background to understanding the development of
investigative journalism in the region. Generally speaking, journalism in Arab states
operated in an environment of state governance and hegemony. Ayalon explains:


The ruler was expected to govern effectively, and it was desirable that he
be just; the ruled were required to obey him, regardless of the quality of
his justice. The basic principle underlying this concept was that
effective government was essential for the stability and proper
functioning of the community, and obedience was vital in assuring such
effectiveness (Ayalon, 1995: 109).


The media were therefore regarded as playing a focal role in assuring the
effectiveness of people’s obedience. Arab journalism, therefore, has broadly acted as
the mouthpiece of the state, thus avoiding political, religious and cultural
questioning. The earliest legislation to affect Arab press came under the Ottoman
Law of Printing and Publications in January 1857. This law stipulated the need to
gain permission from the Council of Education and the Ministry of Police in order to
establish a press. Yet what was particularly vital to the notion of free press in this
case, was that this law obliged anybody who had a press licence, to submit all texts
that were intended for publication for council approval. A year later a penal code
imposed punishments, which included closures and fines, to those who did not
submit material for prepublication approval. (Ayalaon, 1995: 111). As Ayalon states,
‘[l]icensing, prepublication censorship, and prescribed punishments—a set of
preventative measures—would become the cornerstone of government control of
printed self-expression by individual subjects’ (Ayalon, 1995: 112). This,
unavoidably, led to journalists’ self-censorship of what to report on and what to
avoid in their publications. They were fully aware of political, religious and social red
lines they were not allowed to cross. Having said this, ‘[t]he rules for what was
permissible or prohibited were seldom consistent, and interpretation depended
largely on the understanding, or ignorance, of the censors. This resulted in endless
ridiculous situations’ (Ayalon, 1995: 114). These situations included, for example,
the monitoring of words that could have double meanings.


Hence throughout the nineteenth and twentieth century, the region’s leaders
‘were irritated not only by the conduct of the press but by its very existence as well.
Freedom of expression, a novel concept and a newly coined phrase in Arabic, was
part of an alien system’ (Ayalon, 1995: 117). Nonetheless, there were Arab free
thinkers and intellectuals, such as Rifa’a Rafi’ Al-Tahtawi, who rose to promote
enlightenment thought in Egyptian society in the 1830s and beyond. There were
those who ‘were drawn to the press under the inspiration of a foreign example [and]
pursued journalism successfully in the 1860s and 1870s’ (Ayalon, 1995: 216). They
‘showed that it was possible to publish opinions on public affairs and have an impact
without antagonizing the government beyond an acceptable point, produce a
saleable commodity, and even make a living out of it—in other words, to turn writing
on current affairs into a career’ (Ayalon, 1995: 216).


9 3


The development of the profession itself, therefore, began with the introduction
of Western concepts and teachings of journalism into Arab journalism. Such
concepts included notions of objectivity and fairness. Yet within such a tightly
monitored Arab media environment such concepts were not always easy to apply.
This meant that Arab journalism was forced to develop in a different way to that of
Western journalism. Accordingly, Arab ‘journalism developed as a variation distinct
from the European prototype in the social and political roles it filled’ (Ayalon, 1995:
215). Prominent journalists who rose to become influential included Mustafa Kamil,
Ahmad Lutfi Al-Sayyid, Muhammad Al-Tabi’i, Amin Al-Rafi’i, Mohammad Hassanain
Heikal, Abbas Mahmuod Al-Akkad, Sa’d and Fathi Zaghlul, Abdul Qadir Hamza, and
Mahmuod Azmi from Egypt; Jibran and Ghassan Tuwayni, and Muhi Al-Din
Al-Nusuli from Lebanon; the Rayyis cousins, Muhammad Kurd Ali, and Yusuf Al-Isa,
and Nassuh Babil from Syria; Tawfiq Al-Sam’ani and Rafa’il Batti from Iraq; and Isa
Al-Isa from Palestine (Ayalon, 1995: 217). The number of newspapers established by
the end of 1929 rose to three thousand Arabic periodicals (Ayalon, 1995: 217). At the
time, there were minimum requirements for setting up a non-governmental private
newspaper, whereby licence conditions would include a minimum age of 20-25
years, no criminal record, and financial guarantee (Ayalon, 1995: 217). In some
cases, ‘a writer would establish a paper, and slowly, as other like-minded people were
attracted to it, they formed a party that became active on the political scene’ (Rugh,
2004: 148), especially during French and British rule. Later in the century, however,
there were numerous restrictions which meant that only the state had access to the
media. Currently, the media space is opening up within the Arab world with the
advent of the internet, however independent news websites are continuously being
closed down as witnessed during and post the recent Arab Spring uprisings.


One interesting aspect to Arab journalism was that the culture of journalism at
the outset was focused on the ‘desire to satisfy literary cravings’, and writers
regarded newspapers as an outlet to produce literary work (Ayalon, 1995: 219). The
explains why Arab journalism today is literary in its nature, and why producing
investigative journalism which relies on facts, simple language, and is straight to the
point, is one of the challenges that trainers are facing with investigative journalists
today. The literary expressions in Arab journalism were fuelled further with political
motivation, especially in the face of French and British imperialism of Arab states
that came about as a result of the Sykes-Picot Agreement to partition the Arab world
in 1918 after the defeat of the Ottoman empire. However, ‘the British were more
tolerant of partisan papers than of organized parties’ (Rugh, 2004: 148), and
therefore in some regions of the Arab world journalism played a strong political role
against foreign imperialism. For example, before 1945 in Egypt, the Wafd party
utilised its newspaper as a political tool against the British military, as it was not
possible to challenge the occupation through military means (Rugh, 2004: 149).
Rugh notes that ‘[e]xcept for the interlude of wartime censorship, the press was able
to criticize policies, expose corruption in government, and reflect diverse
philosophies and interests’ (Rugh, 2004: 149). Journalists, therefore, became
idealists in their work, where there was a keen ‘desire to serve the community, the
state, and the homeland’ (Ayalon, 1995: 219). Ayalon explains this further:


Virtually every opening statement of each new journal (and, for that
matter, every journalist’s memoirs) contained the cliche: a desire to
serve the public good. Yet in spite of the commonplace usage of such
formulas, there is no reason to doubt their sincerity (Ayalon, 1995:
219).


Accordingly, as Ayalon describes, journalists during this time became active agents
of cultural and social change (Ayalon, 1995: 216). After the British and French rule
ended in the second half of the twentieth century, however, censorship was once
again imposed on Arab media.


9 4


Investigative reporting in the Arab world is not new. In the past there have been
individual investigations that were carried out by a few reporters, and although most
often they were not labelled as ‘investigative’, they were seen as attempts in
liberalising the press and revolutionising traditional methods of reporting. Such
examples include the work of the Amin Brothers in the 1930s and 1940s in Egypt.
Mustafa Amin and Ali Amin, who were twin brothers, were ‘advocates of
Western-style democracy’, Western liberalism, free enterprise, and free press, that
would help liberalise Egypt (Jehl, 1997). Prior to the nationalising of Egypt’s press in
1960 by President Gamal Abdul Nasser, the Amin brothers were publishers of five
publications, which included Al Akhbar and the weekly Akhbar Al Youm. Their
journalism critiqued those in power, which led to frequent imprisonment. Mustafa
Amin was imprisoned in 1939 for criticising King Farouk, and in the early 1950s
during Nasser’s rule (Jehl, 1997).


Ihsan Abdul Quddoos was also prominent in writing investigative stories for the
publication Rose Al Yusif, which was founded by his mother Fatima al Youssef. He
syndicated columns for government newspapers and magazines. His column,
entitled At a Café on Politics Street, addressed current issues circulating in cafes in
Egypt at the time, thus extending discourses from the physical public sphere to that
of the mediated public sphere. In the 1960s and 1970s, he was also editor of Akhbar
Al Yawm and Al Ahram newspapers (New York Times, 16 January 1990).


Mohammad Hassanain Heikal was another Egyptian journalist who was
influential in his investigative reporting. He was editor-in-chief of the Cairo-based
newspaper Al-Ahram, was keen on developing a tradition and practice of
investigative reporting at the paper, and therefore would train graduates in
investigative journalism and employ them. Yet, unlike the Amin brothers, Heikal had
strong ties with President Gamal Abdel Nasser and therefore gained access to
information. Accordingly, he had consent from authorities to publish his work, in
addition to his investigations. However, during the rule of Anwar Sadat following
Nasser, Heikal continued to report yet ‘when he spoke out too obviously in opposition
to government policy he simply lost his platform’ (Rugh, 2004: 153).


Recent individual attempts at investigative reporting have also existed. An
example of this is the work of Rana Al-Husseini in Jordan who set out to uncover
honour killings and bring them to the attention of the mediated public sphere. This
was significant given this was a taboo topic to be addressed and critiqued in public.
Working for the Jordan Times, Husseini would visit various police stations and
inquire, follow up and uncover various cases of honour crimes, investigate why they
were done and how they were dealt with. Through a series of investigative stories
that created social awareness to the issue, she exposed the legal deficiencies in
dealing with this social practice, thus calling for a legislation reform. As a result, a
National Jordanian Committee was formed to eliminate crimes of honour, and
managed to collect 15,000 signatures in the form of a petition demanding stronger
punishment for honour crimes (Husseini, 9 January 2015). Between 1999 and
2003, amendments to the law were presented to the Jordanian parliament but have
been rejected a number of times. Despite not achieving a concrete change in this
case, Husseini’s work brought the magnitude of the problem to local and global
attention.


9 5


It is necessary to point out that all the historic and recent individual cases of
investigative reporting were isolated, and not sustainable, since there were no
institutionalised units for investigative reporters. Nonetheless, media organisations
in the Arab world now understand the importance of investigative journalism and
are giving it some attention. The reason behind this is because they can see the
effects of investigative reporting and understand its power. In recent times, there has
been a renewal of investigative reporting through media institutions such as Al
Jazeera. Yosri Fouda, who was Al Jazeera’s chief investigative correspondent,
conducted investigative stories through the programme Sirri lil-Ghaya, translated to
‘Top Secret’. Fouda tells how the programme began:


After the launch of Al Jazeera in 1996, I summoned my courage and put
an “outlandish” idea across to its management: to be allowed to
disappear for two months every other month in return for a bi-monthly,
45-minute investigative piece. Sarcastically, the norm in Arab TV then
was that you were only allowed to disappear for 45 minutes if you
promised to come back with two months’ worth of rushes. Rather
expectedly, the proposal generated some kind-hearted laughs and I
almost entered yet another cycle of professional depression (Fouda,
2009).


Despite this rough start, the programme began its broadcasts in 1998 and ran for
ten years. In June 2010, Al Jazeera announced the establishment of its investigative
unit. The Director General at the time, Wadah Khanfar, said this was a:


groundbreaking initiative, at a time major news networks [were]
retreating from investigative journalism the new unit represents an
important step forward for the Network, not only to expand the breadth
of our reporting, but also to drive further into stories for deeper
narratives (Press Release, 29 June 2010).


The unit consisted of three investigative teams whose aim was to conduct their own
investigations, and also commission stories from external sources for output across
all Al Jazeera channels, platforms and languages (Press Release, 29 June 2010).
Another Al Jazeera investigative unit was established in April 2013 as part of Al
Jazeera America, and in January 2011, the network launched the Al Jazeera
Transparency Unit (AJTU), which ‘aims to mobilize its audience - both in the Arab
world and further afield - to submit all forms of content (documents, photos, audio
& video clips, as well as “story tips”) for editorial review and, if merited, online
broadcast and transmission on our English and Arabic-language broadcasts’ (Al
Jazeera Investigates, 9 January 2015). Al Jazeera’s investigative unit deals with
regional and global issues, and it is necessary to note that the investigations it
conducts are therefore conducted at a regional and global level in contrast to the
training and reporting conducted at a more local state level, such as the investigative
units that have been set up with the assistance of the Arab Reporters for
Investigative Journalism (ARIJ).


9 6

Chapter 9

### **The ethics of** **investigative** **reporting:**

**Bias, Sourcing, Conflict of Interest,**
**and Undercover Work**


Advance reading:


Code of Ethics for the national society of journalists.


The class should also be assigned the Society of Professional Journalists
“Code of Ethics”, downloadable at: http://www.spj.org/ethicscode.asp?mobile=no


Carol Kerbage, “The battles for ‘Bab Tabbaneh’ and ‘Jabal Mohsen’: Banter
and Barter, and Bullets”. Arabic: http://arij.net/node/6375 English:
h t t p : / / a r i j . n e t / e n / b a t t l e s - % E 2 % 8 0 % 9 C b a b - t a b b a n e h%E2%80%9D-and-%E2%80%9Cjabal-mohsen%E2%80%9D-banter-and-ba
rter-and-bullets


Seymour Hersh, “Torture at Abu Ghraib”, The New Yorker, May 10 2004, at
http://www.newyorker.com/archive/2004/05/10/040510fa_fact?currentPage=all


Hanène Zbiss, “Quranic Kindergartens in Tunisia: Breeding a Wahhabi Elite”.
Via http://arij.net/en/quranic-kindergartens-tunisia-breeding-wahhabi-elite


0 3


9 9


The ethical issues facing investigative reporters are often less black and white, than
gray. Our stance is that rather than try to discern the ideal or normative ethical
position in a given situation, reporters may find it simpler and more effective to
consider the risks involved. What dangers may arise, for the reporter and for others,
from a given course of action? How can the reporter manage, reduce or eliminate
those risks?


The key ethical risks that face investigative reporters involve three main themes:
revelation, sourcing and conflicts of interest. Let us consider all three from the
standpoint of risk.


**A. The implications of revelation**


1. Reporters are blamed for the truth


Journalists (or for that matter, artists) who reveal injustice, corruption or simple
incompetence are always accused of undermining progress and public order. By
raising awareness of problems, according to this argument, they create even worse
problems.


Thus in the 1980s, journalists who exposed the corruption of the ruling Socialist
Party in France were accused of helping the neo-fascist National Front party to
progress, and in the 1990s, the revelation that a government agency had knowingly
sold blood products contaminated with AIDs to hemophiliacs was denounced as an
attempt to destroy the national health system, motivated by sympathy for the
extreme right. _1_


The instructor will no doubt know of similar cases, in which reporters are accused of
acting against the national interest by exposing injustice, closer to home. If such a
case has been documented in the press or the scholarly literature, the instructor
may copy the report and give it to students as advance reading. If not, the instructor
may use “Ethical Conflict and Investigative Reporting” (Hunter 1997) as advance
reading. _2_


The instructor can address this issue by presenting students with the story of a
recent scandal in their country, and asking:


_Should this be revealed? Or is it better to leave the story untold? What stories should_
_not be told?_


The instructor can offer examples – say, corruption, marital violence, or poor
government services – until someone is willing to say that it does no good to tell the
story.


_1_
_See Hunter, Mark. "Ethical Conflict and Investigative Reporting Le Monde and the Contaminated Blood Affair._
_" The Harvard International Journal of Press/Politics2.2 (1997): 77-95._

_2_
_Ibid._


Rather than ask why, the instructor can ask: _Is the problem real?_


The instructor can then ask: _How will the problem be solved if no one acknowledges_
_that it exists?_


If students say that whether or not a problem is revealed, nothing will change, the
instructor may ask: _Who benefits most when we denounce injustice?_


The answer is: the victims. Whether or not something changes, they take comfort
from knowing that they are not alone. This is the minimum result of an investigation
into injustice or corruption, and it fully justifies telling a story that evokes
corruption, incompetence, social or governance problems. Recall that in his preface
to Story-Based Inquiry, Yosri Fouda says that nothing is sweeter to an investigator
than when someone says, “Thank you for showing the story.” Many of those who say
it are victims who have given up hope of recognition, let alone justice.


The instructor can then ask:


_Should the reporter only show the problem, or should the reporter also propose_
_solutions?_


This question addresses the core of the practice of objectivity: An objective reporter
must not take sides. The ethical underpinning is that the reporter cannot be fair to
a subject if he or she has a stake in the outcome. The reporter’s only goal, by this
theory, is to report the facts, fully and fairly. The reporter must then allow the public
to conclude what the story means, and in particular, what the story shows must
change, and how to do it. This has been called the “mobilisation model” of how
investigative reporting achieves results and sets public agendas (Protess et al.,
1991).  It proposes that the reporter’s revelations of unknown or unappreciated facts _3_
arouses public opinion and desire for reform, and institutional actors – regulators,
prosecutors, legislators, executives – act to satisfy that desire. We will say more
about this later. You can tell your students that this is why, at the end of their book,
_All the President’s Men_, Woodward and Bernstein do not say explicitly that President
Richard Nixon must resign or be impeached and thrown out of office; if they do, they
will be taking sides. Some of the public will think so anyway, but they must try to
keep a distance from all sides in any case.


However, there is another tradition in investigative reporting. It is exemplified by
Albert Londres, the French grand reporter of the post-World War I era, who after
exploring the penal colony of Guyana, closed his series with a demand for four
specific reforms: _“I am done. The government must start,”_ he wrote. All four of
Londres’s proposed reforms eventually became law. The Muckrakers also sought
specific reforms. This tradition goes beyond denouncing injustice, to propose
changes.


_3_
_We consider this the single most important theoretical text ever written about the relationship of investigative_
_journalism to society, and how it affects society._


1 0 0


1 0 1


In the 1990s, some scholars argued that proposing solutions to contemporary
problems is a core mission of news media.  We agree, on condition that the solutions _4_
are supported by serious study and investigation of alternatives (and not merely to
support government or a party, as is commonly done by tame journalists). This
approach is also compatible with current developments such as the entry of large
NGOs, notably Greenpeace, into investigative journalism. Greenpeace does not
merely tell the world that the environment has problems; it actively researches and
promotes solutions to those problems.


We realise that many students will confuse their role with that of activists or
advocates. The instructor can make several points when this confusion arises:


Reporters who propose solutions must be transparent about their interests
and objectives for a given issue.


They inevitably assume responsibility for the consequences of their work, for
better or worse.


Conversely, reporters are frequently accused of negative effects on the
societies where they work, whether those effects are real or not, intended or
not.


Investigative reporters must never be merely the servants of a cause. They
must first seek to discover the truth of a given situation. Positive change
cannot be founded on illusions, no matter how noble they may seem.


The reporter may learn of possible solutions from sources, or imagine them,
then verify whether or not they are workable. This is a worthy goal, and a fair,
thorough investigation allows one to attain it.


2. Investigative revelations may hurt someone


Investigative reports frequently identify specific individuals as responsible for
particular injustices or wrongdoing. These people may suffer consequences: They
can lose their jobs, or their business can lose customers. They may face social anger
or criticism. They may also be prosecuted, if their wrongdoing involves crimes.


The instructor will know of such a story in his or her region. It can be evoked with
students. The instructor may ask: _Under what conditions should we denounce_
_someone? What can justify the damage that we may do to someone’s reputation or_
_livelihood?_


_Is it enough justification that we do not like someone?_

_Is revenge a valid motive?_


_4_
_Rosen, Jay, and Davis Merritt, eds. Public journalism: Theory and practice. Kettering Foundation, 1994._


Students may ask if avenging injustice is mere revenge. Anger at injustice is a valid
source of motivation; but the reporter must not work only to satisfy anger. The
reporter must look coldly at the evidence, whether he or she likes it or not. Seymour
Hersh told Woodward and Bernstein that he was going to “get Kissinger,” but he
would do it “cleanly”. Put another way, you do not allow anger to take you beyond
what the facts will justify, and you do not leave out facts in order to justify your
anger.


Here are some questions you can discuss with your students. Always ask them to
give you an example to support their opinion. If the example is not a fact – if no one
said it, or wrote it, or was seen to do it – then ask them to think of another example
while you ask someone else for theirs.


_What if they are harming others?_


_Are they doing harm deliberately, or by mistake?_


_Have they tried to conceal their acts, which makes it more difficult to repair the_
_damage they have caused?_


_Do they hold a position of responsibility that requires a higher than usual standard of_
_conduct from them? Are they leaders of organisations on whom others depend, for_
_example a company that sells stock to the public, or a member of Parliament?_


To show how these questions are answered by a master reporter, the instructor can
discuss “Torture at Abu Ghraib”, by Seymour Hersh. In this story about torture in an
Iraqi prison under American military control, Hersh begins by discussing the
criminal acts, but he does not stop with this shocking material. He focuses his most
intense attention on the commanders and policy makers who created the situation.
This is a high target, and he hits it. Thus his greatest effort is given to the greater
evil. This is also an ethical stance. As Andrew Jennings says: _Who would you rather_
_discomfort? The guy in the Gulfstream jet or the one on a bike? 5_


**B. Sourcing is an ethical process**


1. Sources have the right to reply


In investigative work, as in all reporting (see the SPJ “Code of Ethics”), anyone who
is implicated in a story as an actor or target must be given the opportunity to reply
to any questions raised by the story, and to explain any facts concerning him or her.
The only exception is when speaking with the source will result in immediate
physical harm to the reporter. However, even gangsters can be interviewed, as the
Organised Crime and Corruption Reporting Project (occrp.org) has proven. _6_


In general, student work which does not include interviews with individuals accused
of wrongdoing or incompetence should be graded unacceptable.


_5_
_Casebook, p. 223._


_6_
_See, for example, Stevan Doj_ č _inovi_ ć _, Bojana Jovanovi_ ć _and Christoph Zotter, “Serbian PM Was Warned Of Ton_ č _ev’s_
_Mafia Ties.” occrp, July 1 2013, https://reportingproject.net/occrp/index.php/en/ccwatch/cc-watch-indepth/2034_


1 0 2


1 0 3


2. Scoops must be corroborated


Very frequently, reporters are offered “scoops” that are essentially disinformation,
based on the testimony of one or another parties to a scandal, or on a purportedly
“secret” document. In such cases the information should not be used unless the
reporter can verify it independently.


To make this point, you can obtain an unsourced newspaper or blog story
