used in its construction are inconsistent. The terms used should also be
terms that do not appear in the dictionary, to avoid false hits. That is why we
suggest adding a certain character (like “:”) to the end of a term.


Or, the information may be something that a human source told them; in that
case they identify the source by name and title, along with contact
information (phone numbers, email addresses, website etc.). They also give
the date and place of the interview. You may explain to students that by
putting this information next to the source, we ensure that we will always
know how to find the person when we need to ask another question.


1 3 9


Whether the source is a document or an interview, students can now add
extracts pertinent to the story.


Further contacts pertinent to the event can be noted at the bottom of the entry
as LOG:, followed by the content of the later encounter. The reason for
collecting this information is that it enables the reporter to keep track of
exchanges with sources.


If the reporter has an insight about the information provided by the source,
this may be added to the event as NOTE:. You may stress to your students
that these insights can be highly valuable material. We recommend to
students that they always carry a pen and paper in order to capture insights
related to the investigation; then, they can enter the material into their
masterfiles as soon as they have the opportunity.


Finally, if further information must be sought, or some other acton must be
accomplished, the reporter can enter this as TO DO:.


Below is an example from an investigation conducted by Luuk Sengers and
colleagues, in which we have inserted all the categories of data described above. Not
every entry in the masterfile will require every category.


July 2003: Biologists witness chemicals deforming human cells


Interview with Ana Soto, professor in cell biology, Tufts University, Paris, October
12, 2005


CONTACT: +44 34554 662, soto.a@tufts.edu


“I was with two colleagues engaged in research into the impact of estrogen –
female hormones – on the development of breast cancer. We knew that cancer
cells proliferate in test tubes when they are brought into contact with estrogen.
But to our surprise, exactly the same thing happened in control tubes where no
estrogen had been added. Unbelievable!


“Like Sherlock Holmes we went looking for the perpetrator. Carefully, we studied
all testing phases and checked all equipment. After months of tracking, we
discovered the cause: the estrogens came from our plastic test tubes!”
LOG: 11/1/2005 mail to confirm interview extracts.


“Phtalates in infants”, in: Environmental Health Perspectives, Issue 5, May 2005
“Even normal exposure to plasticizers may interfere with development of the
genitals in male infants. Mothers with high concentrations of phthalates in their
urine during pregnancy often gave birth to boys with an incomplete
masculinization.”


NOTE: This was the first time that a scientist confirmed the link between the
plastics used in water pipes and specific chemicals that may be harmful to
human health.


TO DO: Search scientific databases for further studies.


1 4 0


As the investigation proceeds, students will discover more events related to their
subject. Each is inserted into the timeline at the appropriate place, in as much detail
as necessary. The initial timeline will expand to include these new events. Some of
the events we hypothesised will turn out to be different, or will be replaced by others.
Some events we never imagined will appear. We will see the story unfolding before
our eyes, and we will capture the facts that prove it.


This is not an exact art. Students will learn through experience how much material
they need on particular points in order to understand and compose a given story.
What they cannot avoid is the process of setting their material in a provisional order,
along with proof of its verification, _as they are collecting it._


III. Exercise (20 minutes)


Once you feel that the students have captured the basic principles, ask them to take
15 minutes and enter sources, insights, notes or actions to accomplish into the
timelines of their story proposals. If they know of further events (such as a
conversation with a source) or data (including ideas) that came into their possession
since the proposal was written, ask them to add it in. Then quickly review their work
to see if they are keeping the material in order. They must understand that a
masterfile is a path, not a garbage bin.


IV. Frequently asked questions (20 minutes)


Students often ask questions about this process – or think of the questions, but
don’t dare to ask. These are the most frequent, along with the answers:


Isn’t this a lot of extra work?


While not every journalist uses this method, every top-level professional in the world
uses some method of organising. If they wish to do the extra work, invite them to
read the Afterwords in the _Casebook_, and see if there is another method that suits
them better. You may ask them to present it to the class for discussion.


You can also tell students that making a database will save you many hours of work,
and much anxiety. Sooner or later, students will enter their material in an electronic
file, if only to write the story. Later, we will show them that by putting the material in
an electronic file as soon as they collect it, the writing process will become much
easier. It will also be less worrisome, because students will see how they discovered
a given fact, and will feel more confident. Not least, it will enable you to feel more
confident about your students’ work, too.


How much of an interview or a document should we extract into the file?


Students can put as much of these sources into the file as they like – whatever may
be of value. When it is time to write, they can synthesise or cut the unnecessary
material.


Very frequently, source documents are available in electronic form. They can be
stocked on a hard drive or other media. Students may create a hyperlink from the
descriptions of documents to the saved documents. That way you can quickly go
from the masterfile to the original document if they want to check something


1 4 1


What about confidential sources? Should they be in the database?


This is a very good question, and the answer is: Never put anything on your
computer that might compromise a confidential source. Confidential human
sources should be referred to by code names, and confidential documents (including
interview transcripts) can be kept elsewhere – for example, in an online depository,
behind a strong password.


What about databases of statistics or tables? Do we put them in too?


If they fit, why not? Otherwise summarise extracts and hyperlink to the originals.

You may inform your students that from now on, ideas from every discussion about
their stories should be captured as a NOTE: in the masterfile. You may also advise
them that whether or not they are concentrating on the story, they may come across
information relevant to it. This information, too, can be captured in the masterfile.
Thus they will build their stories, piece by piece, bit by bit, according to the time and
energy at their disposal. And when they return to their stories, what they know will
be neatly organised, so that they can focus on what they do not know yet.


1 4 2


Chapter 13

### **Audiences, Values** **and markets for** **investigative reporting**

**/1.5 hours**


Advance reading for the class:


Hunter, M., & Van Wassenhove, L.N. (2010). Disruptive News Technologies:
Stakeholder Media and the Future of Watchdog Journalism Business
Models. Available as a free download at:
https://flora.insead.edu/fichiersti_wp/inseadwp2010/2010-15.pdf


Lewis, Charles. "The growing importance of nonprofit journalism."
Cambridge, MA: Joan Shorenstein Center on the Press, Politics and Public
Policy, John F. Kennedy School of Government, Harvard University (2007).
At :
http://shorensteincenter.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/03/2007_03_lewis
.pdf


Luesby, J. “Enlightening entrepreneurs”. D+C, Jan. 13 2011. At:
http://www.dandc.eu/en/article/business-daily-young-kenyan-newspaperhelps-improve-economic-fortunes (PDF version can be downloaded.)


Rama Najmeh, “Kidney failure patients in Syria suffer from minimum health
care”. (via ARIJ.net) PLEASE ADD LINK TO STORY IN ARABIC


0 3


In this chapter we will address the theoretical and practical aspects of how
investigative journalism creates great value, and how journalists may capture some
of it.


The fundamental questions that must be asked and answered in this session are:


What value does investigative reporting create?


Who captures that value?


A. The Value created by investigative reporting


1. The public value of exposing injustice


a. Creating an informed citizenry


The classic argument for independent journalism in a democracy was set out by
James Madison, a principal author of the US Constitution. Madison wrote to a
friend: “Knowledge will forever govern Ignorance: And a people who mean to be their
own governors, must arm themselves with the power which knowledge gives.”  As _1_
Charles Lewis, a great contemporary figure in investigative reporting,  puts it: “To _2_
what extent can a democracy… exist without an informed citizenry?”  The answer, _3_
of course, is that without valid information about what leaders are doing (as opposed
to what they say they are doing), there can be no real democracy.


_1_


_2_

_3_


Students may object that even when people know that their rulers are corrupt or
incompetent, they may not be able to do anything about it. They may point to an
exposé of official wrongdoing and say, “This produced no results.” This is, in fact, a
naïve argument.


The instructor can remind students of the coalition model of investigative reporting:
Whether or not there is official action and cooperation in response to an investigative
story, other stakeholders, such as civil society organisations or honest officials, will
take note of it. By becoming sources for reporters, and following their news, these
stakeholders become de facto allies for the reporter’s desired reform.


Conversely, by offering comfort to brave individuals who confront power,
investigative reporters show that they are not alone. If an opportunity later emerges
to fix the situation, these forces may act on it. At the least, it becomes more difficult
for corrupt practices to continue in the face of public exposure. The risks for
wrongdoers rise; their enemies perceive their vulnerabilities; their friends are
ashamed of their company. The wrongdoers may or may not care, especially if they
are powerful enough to silence their enemies. But in Tunisia, in Egypt, and in the
Ukraine, to speak only of examples from recent years, the cost of maintaining power
through corruption and injustice steadily rose, until it became unsustainable. Even
small streams can cut deep holes in mountain rock.


_1_
James Madison to W.T. Barry, 4 August 1822, Writings 9-103. Via
_http://press-pubs.uchicago.edu/founders/documents/v1ch18s35.html_


_2_
Among other things, Lewis founded the Center for Public Integrity (www.publicintegrity.org), one of the first
non-profit investigative journalism institutions, and the International Consortium of Investigative Journalists
(www.icij.org).

_3_


1 4 4


1 4 5


Nobel Prize-winning economist Amartya Sen has argued that independent media
make it more difficult for leaders to commit grave policy errors, or to hide their
consequences. As evidence, he notes that India has escaped its former catastrophic
famines since it gained independence and a free press. In _Development as Freedom_,
Sen observes that “a free press and an active political opposition constitute the best
early-warning system a country threatened by famines can have”. _4_


From all these perspectives, the value created by investigative reporting is first and
foremost social and general. It benefits the entire society before, and more than, it
benefits the media enterprise or the reporter.


b. Value for demographic segments of society


In _All the News That’s Fit to Sell_ (2004), James Hamilton demonstrated that _5_
regardless of the channel, from television to print and from radio to Internet,
different demographic segments of the American news audience retain similar
interests.


The public for news about politics, sports and business is largely (though not
exclusively) masculine.


The public for news about education, health and nutrition is overwhelmingly
female.


This discovery has important marketing implications for investigative reporting in
general, and for your students in particular. In many national markets, investigative
reporting focuses on politics and business. These are important subjects, but they
are hardly the only subjects of interest to the public, and to women in particular.
Historically, magazines or broadcast formats aimed at women have been an
important market for investigative reporters concerned with health and education
issues. Your students should be encouraged to take these media seriously, if they do
not already.


Of course, from a general perspective, injustice and suffering is a concern for
everyone in a society. But from a particular, individual perspective, certain stories,
and certain kinds of stories, matter more to some of us than to others. This is
particularly true for stakeholder groups, as we will discuss below.


c. Value for organisational or institutional stakeholders


As the coalition model suggests, an investigation will typically be of greater urgency,
importance and interest to particular stakeholders in an issue, an institution, an
organisation or a society – for example, as Amartya Sen noted, an active political
opposition. There is nothing wrong or perverse in that fact; not everyone in a given
society cares about the issues that matter to investigative reporters, and not
everyone has the time to care, let alone to do something about those issues.


_4_
Sen, Amartya. Development as freedom. Oxford University Press, 1999.


_5_
Hamilton, James. All the news that's fit to sell: How the market transforms information into news.


The instructor can make this point through the story, “Kidney failure patients in
Syria suffer from minimum health care”. Written before the Syrian civil war began,
the story tells how insufficient investment in hospital equipment condemns patients
to dreadful suffering and premature death.


The instructor can ask: _Assume that this story takes place in a country where there is_
_no civil war. Who will be interested in this story? Do not tell me ‘everyone.’ Who, in_
_particular, will be interested?_


Appropriate answers include:


People who suffer from renal disorders, and their families, employers and
friends;


medical personnel who must treat these cases, knowing that they can only
end badly;


political leaders who want to avoid scandal;


and personnel at the Ministry of Health who must make budget decisions,
and who may or may not agree with how their budget is spent.


The instructor can then ask: _How might the story change their situations?_


Appropriate answers include:


By making comparisons with other countries, the story shows clearly that
Syria has not invested appropriately.


By attracting attention to how the Ministry spends its budget, the story
strengthens advocates for different priorities (in particular, doctors), and puts
the Ministry on the defensive. The Ministry can no longer claim that it has
done its best to solve this problem.


If the problem recurs in the future, those responsible for solving it cannot
claim, “We didn’t know.”


In the contemporary era, online news media often exemplify a stakeholder-based
approach to creating value for their users. The instructor will know which online
media, in his or her country, represents particular stakeholder groups. Often these
groups are focused on a particular domain, such as health, governance, or business.
If these media are absent, that may represent a career option for your students.


Increasingly, non-governmental organisations (NGOs) conduct investigative research
on the issues that concern them. Often, they employ journalists to help research
and write their reports. This is a legitimate form of employment for journalists, on
condition that they recognise the risk of a conflict of interest toward their employers
as well as their targets.


You may ask your students to research NGOs that have published reports
concerning your country, and to analyse the reports: What sources do they appear to
be based on? Can they be verified? Are they well-written or filmed, from a journalistic
standpoint? Representatives of NGOs may be considered as possible guest speakers
in the class. If so, the focus should be on their research methods, and on how they
work with journalists.


1 4 6


1 4 7


2. The private economic value of investigative stories


Investigative reporting can also create economic value for individuals or enterprises,
as well as for society. This principle is deeply embedded in the history of journalism.


a. Investigative news and business advantage


If the value created by investigative reporting is to be general, then a report must in
theory be available to everyone at the same time. In the Internet age, this is indeed
feasible. But in practice, only a minority of the public will be aware of a given report.
There is thus an advantage for those who follow the news, and especially
investigative reporting, on subjects of interest to them. That advantage resides in the
possession of reliable information on which actions and decisions may be based. In
other words, investigative reporting can reduce the risks inherent in any course of
action undertaken by an entrepreneur or an organisation.


The first regular news publications in the English language were 17th-century
newsletters, sold by subscription, which offered information concerning events in
Europe that might affect businesses in England. Subscribers paid high prices for
news that could impact their personal fortunes.  They could hardly rely on official _6_
announcements for that information. Even if their rulers told them the truth, by the
time everyone else knew it there could be no chance to gain commercial advantage.
To this day, newsletters, newspapers or magazines targeted at particular industries
command a premium price in the news marketplace.


Jenny Luesby, a trainer who helped to create the _Business Daily_ of Nairobi, has well
described the cost of not having such information for businesses in the developing
world:


_In most emerging markets, there is hardly any public and palatably packaged_
_information about which markets are growing and which are not. Reliable_
_reporting of prices is scant for everything from raw materials to final goods,_
_from wage rates to rental fees._
_Managers and entrepreneurs in developing countries therefore lack vital_
_information. For them, it is extremely difficult to find out with any precision_

_– which activities are currently making the best profits,_

_– which businesses are growing fast,_

_– which market segments are suffering from over-supply,_

_– who needs new staff urgently or_

_– what new competition from abroad is imminent?_


_3_
Frank, Joseph. The Beginnings of the English Newspaper, 1620-1660. Harvard U. Press, 1961.


Before the Business Daily existed, no one covered such issues in Nairobi. Luesby
argues that this information vacuum is one reason that Chinese producers seized a
large share of the international market for Kenyan roses, a key export product. Had
an enterprising reporter spotted the coming threat, Kenyan producers might have
prepared a better strategy. _7_


Students may be shocked by the idea that investigative reporting can serve
commercial interests. If the instructor perceives that they are indeed uncomfortable,
he or she can ask: _Why is it wrong to provide social actors, including businesspeople,_
_with valid information that can affect their prosperity?_ The instructor may also note
that so-called “trade”media may represent a paying market for first-rate reports. The
much-admired Nambian investigative journalist, John Grobler, has supported his
work by writing about energy issues in Africa for newsletters aimed at actors in that
industry. _8_


b. Investigative reporting and consumer protection


Another way that investigative reporting can have great economic impact for
particular individuals is by helping them to avoid frauds and thieves. Barry Yeoman’s
award-winning investigation of fraudulent professional schools, which cheat their
students by providing sub-standard education at high prices, leaving them indebted
and desperate, shows individuals how to avoid becoming victims.  (It is worth noting _9_
that Yeoman’s story can be replicated in virtually every country of the world; you may
suggest such a project to your students.)To take another example, _Business Daily_
took a leading role in exposing corruption at the Nairobi Stock Exchange. Thousands
of investors had been ruined by crooked brokerage practices, discouraging
investment even in well-run businesses. Without _Business Daily’s_ relentless
investigation of the scandal, authorities would probably have continued to tolerate
the situation.


Investigative reporting on consumer issues – such as defective products, fraudulent
lending practices, or services (including health and education) that are paid for but
never provided – will remain a major genre of news reporting. Particularly in
developing countries, there remains much to do in this area. Students may find this
work less attractive or prestigious than exposés of corruption or injustice. But
consumers are victims of much injustice. Moreover, by doing this work, reporters can
make their audiences’ lives better right away. Their publics will remember. The
instructor can use this moment in the discussion to suggest that at least some
students undertake investigative projects concerned with consumer issues.


_7_
Luesby, J., “Enlightening entrepreneurs”. D+C, Jan. 13 2011, at :
_http://www.dandc.eu/en/article/business-daily-young-kenyan-newspaper-helps-improve-economic-fortunes_


_8_
For an example of Grobler’s recent work, see
_http://www.journalism.co.za/blog/angolas-chinese-built-rail-link-scramble-access-regions-resources/_


_9_
See Yeoman, Barry, “The School of Hard Knocks”. Casebook, pp. 28-40.


_10_
Mark Lee Hunter and Luk N Van Wassenhove "The Nation Media Group (B): Launching the Business Daily "


1 4 8


1 4 9


Investigative consumer reporting need not focus only on scandals to create value for
its audiences. Though there are over 14 million Internet sites in English that offer
visitors “free stuff” (according to a Google search), few of these sites verify whether or
not the offers are truly valuable. A French website, http://www.lesradins.com, did
just that. Its audience saved not only money that might have been spent on buying
competing products, but also the “search time” necessary to find valid free offers. _11_


The instructor can remind students that investigative work means getting and
verifying information, through one’s own efforts, that was not simply provided,
unsolicited, by a source. It is not only about revealing secrets. By providing our
audience with information that they cannot verify for themselves, we create value for
them. In the process, we make it easier for the media that publish our work to
capture value, through advertising, sponsorship, circulation or other revenues. We
discuss this point further below.


3. Media independence as a business asset


a. The classic model: “good journalism is good business”


The classic model of how investigative journalism creates economic value for the
profession was defined by James Breiner, a leading researcher of business models
for news media:


_When I was editor of Business First of Columbus (Ohio, USA), our paper did an_
_investigative story about a bank’s behind-the-scenes manipulations to get the_
_state to take over a failed office project. The stories scuttled the deal and_
_caused the bank, our biggest advertiser, to cancel its contract._


_The reaction of Publisher Carole Williams to the cancelled contract set an_
_example for me and gave me words to live by. The lost revenue would hurt us,_
_she said, but would not result in cutbacks at the paper. We had other_
_advertisers. Investigative stories strengthened our credibility and made_
_advertisers want to be associated with us, she said. In other words, good_
_journalism was good business. In turn, profitability safeguarded our editorial_
_independence._ _[12]_


This argument is not only American, as some students may object. It has been
confirmed by studies of the Kiev Post in Ukraine  and Nation Media Group in Kenya. _13_
In general, every media market supports at least one source of reliable information
for each significant stakeholder group in the market. The reason is that without this
information, it is very difficult for various actors to conduct their affairs. Thus, while
specific stakeholders may become angry with a particular investigative report, others
will recognise the necessity for such work, on condition that it is conducted fairly and
professionally.


_11_
_Hunter, M.L. and Van Wassenhove, L.N., Disruptive News Technologies: Stakeholder Media and the Future_


_of Watchdog Journalism Business Models. INSEAD, 2010, pp. 20-22._


_12_
_http://newsentrepreneurs.blogspot.com/2011/04/5-dirty-words-journalists-have-to-learn.html_


In contrast, informed stakeholders will quickly be aware when investigative reports
are used to obtain partisan or personal advantage. They may continue to read or
watch media that do so, but they will no longer support it or believe it to the same
extent. This represents a loss of long-term value for the media, its reporters and
owners. For example:


The Nation Media Group of Kenya, founded in the 1960s as the voice of
Kenya’s masses, rapidly gained greater circulation than its historic rivals who
supported colonial-era powers. Yet in the 1980s, the group’s bizarre failure to
properly investigate a political assassination cost it both reputation and
circulation. In the 1990s, its success in exposing a financial scandal that
nearly wrecked the Kenyan economy re-established its pre-eminence in
Kenya, which the journal has since maintained. The NMG currently accounts
for 70 % of all newspaper sales in Kenya, and has been profitable for over a
decade. _14_


b. If good journalism is good business, and investigative reporting is good
journalism, why do people say it’s bad for business?


Many publishers and editors argue – often on the basis of little, if any experience –
that investigative reporting is too slow, expensive and risky for their media to
undertake. This argument is made in every country we know of. Your students may
well have heard it; they may be afraid that investigation will take more time and
other resources than they can possibly afford. You can address that fear, and it may
be helpful to know what is behind it.


The argument that investigation is always slow, uncertain and costly first emerged
in the post-Watergate period, when the great majority of investigative reporters,
editors and publishers hardly knew what they were doing. In the process of learning
their craft, they increased their costs and risks in numerous ways. These failings are
still obvious whenever investigative reporting begins to be practiced in a region
where it was previously unknown:


Inefficient processes lead to vast amounts of wasted time. In particular,
reporters compile massive amounts of information about institutions or
individuals without first defining a hypothesis that would enable them to
focus on viable stories.


Poor information management creates legal risks. Reporters in a hurry
often make grave mistakes, because they have not understood what sources
are telling them; this happened to Woodward and Bernstein during the
Watergate investigation, and nearly killed their story. From another
perspective, few reporters are trained in archiving techniques that could
enable them to control and catalogue the information they collected.
Consequently, they are only partly aware, or not aware at all, of the gaps in
their documentation. They are thus exposed to damages in the event that
someone sued them.


_14_
Loughran, G. (2010). Birth of a Nation: the story of a newspaper in Kenya. IB Tauris.


1 5 0


1 5 1


Opportunity costs – that is, the foregone profits from not being able to
seize an opportunity – rise when investigations waste resources. To take
one example, most of the data and documentation that reporters acquire is
either never catalogued, or thrown away after a project was completed. Thus
valuable information that could be re-packaged for consulting or further
publication was discarded.


Promotional strategies based on the mobilisation model, rather than the
coalition model, limit the branding, political and social benefits of
investigative projects. Investigative media often hope that simply
publishing a story will create sufficient outrage to achieve reform. The idea
is that brave publishers and reporters, acting on their own, can incite the
public to action. You can remind your students that numerous studies have
confirmed that the success of Woodward and Bernstein depended on a
growing coalition of forces, including other news media, the Federal courts,
legislators, progressive business interests, and others. In the contemporary
era, the Contaminated Blood Scandal in France and the _News of the World_
scandal in the UK were triumphs for brave investigative reporters, but those
reporters also deliberately built bridges to victims, lawyers, honest
government officials and others.


It is therefore more accurate to say that when badly managed as a business
investment and as a coalition for reform, investigative reporting delivers low or
negative returns; but when properly managed, media enterprises derive direct
and indirect profits from investigative reporting.


When properly managed, returns on investigative news media can be very high:


KP Media gained both advertising and political support for its print and
online media, as the best independent source of information in the Ukrainian
market. From an initial investment of $US 8000 in 1994, the firm was valued
at $55 million on the Kiev stock exchange in 2006. _16_


The most profitable broadcast product in the history of the television
industry, the CBS show _60 Minutes_, based a large share of its appeal to
audiences and advertisers on investigative stories.


The _Canard enchaîné_, a weekly newspaper in France, is not only an
influential actor in French society; it is also highly profitable. In recent years
the Canard has generated margins of about 15 % on revenues of nearly 50
million euros. Of particular note is that all of those revenues are from
circulation; the publication accepts no advertising. _17_


_15_
_Watson, T., & Hickman, M. (2012). Dial M for Murdoch: News Corporation and the corruption of Britain. Penguin UK._


_16_
_KP Media A_


_17_
_Disruptive News Technologies, pp. 16-20._


Beginning in 1995, Greenpeace sought to establish itself not only as a leading
activist organisation (which it already was), but also as the world’s best
source of verified information on environmental issues.  The organisation is _18_
entirely supported by donations from its three million members. Its website
includes numerous investigative reports, which the instructor can survey
and assign to students as advance reading if desired. The organisation
spends approximately €10 million annually on its investigations. _19_


_18_


_19_


The Economist Group has established a profitable strategy based on
leveraging verified, exclusive and accessible information across different
formats. Its weekly magazine, The Economist, is available both in print and
online, and both formats are profitable. So is the group’s magazine for
government leaders, CQ Roll Call, and its Intelligence unit, which provides
in-depth reports on countries and markets to businesses and institutions.
Other products and services include conferences, education, etc. The
instructor can, if desired, download annual reports from the Group’s website
and use them to demonstrate how reliable, independently verified
information generates profits. _20_


c. The price of corruption in journalism


Anecdotal but exemplary evidence suggests that regardless of a media’s reputation
or investigative excellence, corruption elsewhere in the format will impact its brand,
effectively wasting the value of investment in investigations. An example:


In France, the corruption of _Le Monde_ ’s medical and culture pages in the late
1980s, as section editors indulged in conflicts of interest with their sources,
was the precursor to the rapid decline of the entire journal. As we have seen,
the journal’s medical reporter was compromised in the Contaminated Blood
Affair. Simultaneously, the editor of the culture pages took a highly partisan
stance in favour of the Left’s arts policies, while also writing publicity
packages for films that were favourably covered in the journal. Meanwhile, on
several occasions _Le Monde_ ’s investigative reporting became an instrument of
personal vengeance. A decade was required to set a new course, during which
the journal lost revenues and readers. _21_


Your students may argue that certain popular and profitable media in their country
or region are visibly corrupt. If they do, propose to them that they investigate these
media, to understand how they can be profitable. In general, they will discover that
these media are not profitable, after all. They are subsidised by their owners, or
political interests, for propaganda or influence purposes. The public ends to despise
such media. Under Mubarak, one government-subsidised Egyptian daily newspaper
was printed in thousands of copies, but could count its readers in the hundreds.


_18_
Disruptive News Technologies, pp. 22-25.


_19_
_http://www.greenpeace.org/international/en/publications/Campaign-reports/_


_20_
_http://www.economistgroup.com/results_and_governance/annual_and_interim_reports.html_


_21_


1 5 2


d. The operational conditions for creating value from investigative reporting


Investigative reporting creates lesser or no value, for society as for journalists, if it is
not properly managed, promoted and distributed. Our case studies suggest the
following principles:


Branding: Investigative reporting adds most and best to the fortunes of a
media enterprise when it is perceived as setting a standard of independence
for the entire format. In other words, it does little good, from a business
standpoint, to publish an occasional investigation if the rest of the format is
corrupt or incompetent.


Professionalism: There must be close management of investigations, to
ensure that they are well conceived and executed, that budgets are respected
(or adapted to the growing scale of a major story), and that return on
investment is defined for key projects. At an enterprise level, return on
investment may be measured through increased audience or advertising
revenues, new skills and capabilities, brand recognition or reputation, new
information or data assets, etc. (We will deal with return on investment for
the individual reporter later in the term, when students are asked to define
projects.)


Promotion: Investigations are not simply “thrown over the wall”. They are
announced in advance, in particular to allies who will support them. Print
runs are increased for exclusive stories. (In the case of the Nation Media
Group, print runs for major investigations are 20 % higher than usual.)


Defense: Hostile response from targets of the investigation is anticipated and
prepared for. Contacts are made with supporting forces in the society, so that
adversaries will find themselves surrounded by watchful monitors. Lawyers
review material before it is published. Management at the media are fully
briefed on the story and can support it.


Students will certainly object that these conditions are met by hardly any
media in their region, if by any at all. That may indeed be true.


But that situation is neither inevitable, inescapable nor eternal. Kenya and the
Ukraine are hardly easier environments for journalists than the Arab states.
Nonetheless, the Nation Media Group and KP Media proved that independent
journalism could thrive in those countries, precisely because readers and viewers
care that their sources of news are independent and credible. They also proved that
investigative capability, and the big, long-running stories it creates, are key to
winning and keeping their public’s attention. Finally, they proved that when other
businesses and stakeholders perceive that a media is fighting for their interests by
opposing corruption and injustice, they will support it with advertising and
circulation investments. The task and glory of your students will be to achieve
similar success in their region.


We will discuss the value of investigative journalism for your students in the next
session.


1 5 3


Chapter 14

### **Investigative** **journalism as** **a social force**

**/1.5 hours**


In this section we analyse how investigative journalism fits into society
and collaborates with other social forces, in order to create and drive
solutions to problems and injustice.


Advance reading (Instructor):
Protess, David, ed. The journalism of outrage: Investigative reporting and
agenda building in America. Guilford Press, 1992.


Advance viewing (instructor and class)
Hana Khandakji, “Abuse at Jordan's private care homes for mentally
disabled kids”,
http://arij.net/en/abuse-jordans-private-care-homes-mentally-disabled-kid
s (English), http://arij.net/node/5439


0 3


**I. How investigation involves other actors**


Prior to the class, the instructor will assign students to watch the investigative report
by Hana Kandakji, “Abuse at Jordan's private care homes for mentally disabled
kids”, at http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=SzVgAitXWdI


Students should be asked to consider the following questions as they watch the film.
Their answers should be short, and in writing, and should be brought to class.


Why is this story important?


Who specifically does the story matter to?


How do we expect each of these parties to react to the story?


The instructor can initiate discussion of these questions as soon as the class begins.
The instructor can inform the class, during the discussion, of the following elements
pertinent to each question:


1.


2.


Why is this story important? _A sufficient answer is: Because it concerns the_
_abuse of helpless victims in schools sanctioned and regulated by the State._
_Answers that refer only to suffering or to victims, without going into detail,_
_are not sufficient._


Who, specifically, does the story matter to? How will they respond to it?
_The minimum answer includes the children, their parents and relatives,_
_workers in the schools, owners of the schools, the regulators who certify and_
_inspect the schools, the ministry that employs the inspectors, and lawyers_
_who may file complaints seeking damages or prosecution for acts committed_
_in the schools._


Your students can deduce all of these elements without further knowledge.


But the instructor can also inform them of some specific actors who were
necessary to doing the story and to defending it after publication. The
instructor can ask after introducing each: _What is their interest in the_
_story?_


1 5 5


_In the research phase:_


A former volunteer worker in schools for handicapped children provided the
names of institutions here children are abused, and that also use volunteers.
This enabled the reporter to identify targets and an identity for undercover
work. The former volunteer later accompanied the reporter into the schools,
and took photographs. _The volunteer’s interest is to end abuses that he or she_
_personally witnessed, and found unacceptable._


The owner of a school where children are treated correctly provided a week’s
training for the reporter in dealing with the handicapped. _The owner’s interest_
_is to show that he is not among the wrongdoers; his business will benefit, and_
_he will feel that his virtue has been rewarded._


Parents of children who attended the schools provided medical reports
and statements. _The parents wish to protect their children and to force_
_exploiters of children out of business._


A legal centre provided knowledge of regulation concerning schools for the
handicapped. _The legal centre wishes to promote its expertise and to influence_
_future regulation and enforcement._


_After publication:_


The King of Jordan read an initial print version of the story, and saw that it
would be followed by a report on the BBC network. The King intervened to
protect the reporter and spur responsible officials to action. As the head of
State, the King is ultimately responsible for the situation, and has the power
to prosecute and reform. It is greatly to his credit that he uses his power
wisely.


The Ministry of Social Affairs asked parents to file complaints, so that they
could investigate and if necessary close certain schools. The Ministry must
satisfy its hierarchical superior, and to do so, needs the help of parents.


Social media learned of the King’s intervention, and the story went viral.
Thanks to the King and the Ministry, the story has become a safe and
legitimate subject of discussion and outrage. It cannot be considered
defamatory, but as a scandal that must and will be solved.


1 5 6


The contemporary term for these different social actors is stakeholders – that is,
people who have an interest in the outcomes from a given organization, issue or
situation. Imagine trying to do this story without them: The reporter would not know
which schools to target, what she would see once she got inside, or how to deal with
handicapped children, without the former volunteer and the virtuous school owner
to train her. Without medical reports she would not be able to prove that children
were injured while at school, and not elsewhere. Without legal assistance she could
not identify ways in which some schools break the law. And without the King and the
Ministry of Social Affairs, no complaints would be filed to enable prosecution.


In sum, social forces enter into the reporter’s work not only or merely as an audience;
they furnish:


sources (notably through introductions),


resources (including labour, information, data, documents),


support (such as help with travel and lodging),


protection (via legal aid or political influence)


and action beyond the reporter’s own means.


We are aware (and professors affiliated with ARIJ inform us) that many students in
the Arab world see themselves as social activists, and not only journalists. In fact,
this issue is universal. It is also revelatory: Students who desire to be activists may
not fully believe in the fundamental value of journalism. They may place a higher
value on activism. In some cases, they may feel inferior to activists, because activists
believe in what they do more strongly than the student believes in his or her work.
Unfortunately, some students are drawn to journalism precisely because they doubt
themselves, and see journalism as a way to enjoy the company of more worthy and
confident people. You cannot help that. However, you can take the opportunity to
discuss the underlying issues.


At this point the instructor may ask:


_Is it part of the reporter’s job to help ensure that the problem she denounces is_
_addressed by authorities and the public?_


We believe, of course, that the answer is yes. The reporter may challenge,
provoke or lead authorities or other social actors to address a given issue.
This leads to a further question:


_Can she achieve that outcome alone?_


Students may argue that it might be possible for a reporter to obtain results
through a single story. If so, the instructor must ask for an example.


1 5 7


There is, in fact, one very common example of the results that a reporter may
obtain simply by revealing a story: Individuals involved in the story feel
grateful that someone has finally told their side of it. But that is not the same
thing as changing their situation, or changing legislation, or putting an end
to corrupt or unjust practices. The reporter can facilitate such outcomes, but
can hardly achieve them alone.


The instructor may make these points, then ask:


_How can the reporter work with other actors in a way that is at once_
_responsible and ethical? To whom is the reporter responsible, and for what?_


Individual students will have different answers to these questions. What
matters most is that they understand that their key responsibility is to
present true information and interpretations to their public and their allies.
They must not place their own objectives or desires above the truth. Whatever
else they do, they must seek to accept and expose the reality of a given
situation. Otherwise, either there will be no change, or the change will be for
the worse. How can positive change be founded on an error or an illusion?


**II. The theory and practice of investigative**
**reporting within society**


A. The Mobilisation and Coalition Models


In their book _The journalism of outrage: Investigative reporting and agenda building in_
_America,_ David L. Protess et al. closely examine how investigative journalism
achieves results – in terms of prosecution, or legislation, or other action that leads to
reform. Their work has never been surpassed. They propose two models that
journalists can use to drive reform:


1.


Mobilisation. The idea is that public outrage at the revelation of scandal
forces political and social leaders to take action. Thus journalists incite the
public to anger, and the public’s anger is heard. Of course, this is the
legend of Watergate. The instructor can remind the class that the legend
is not entirely accurate: Though public outrage at the unfolding scandal
was great, it would not have sufficed unless it also supported journalists,
judges and political leaders who opposed their President.


Yet we cannot simply dismiss the mobilisation model. Prior to Watergate,
the environmentalist Rachel Carson and consumer activist Ralph Nader _1_ _2_
demonstrated the power of journalists engaged in a cause to mobilise
public opinion. Neither was a professional journalist, but both of them
used investigative reporting to persuade the public that their issues
mattered, and both had lasting effects. Among those effects was to
demonstrate to the news industry that environmental and consumer
affairs reporting could be the object of investigative projects.


_1_
For a recent edition see: Carson, Rachel. Silent spring. Houghton Mifflin Harcourt, 2002.


_2_
Nader, Ralph. Unsafe at any speed: The designed-in dangers of the American automobile. (1965)


1 5 8


The mobilisation model began well before the emergence of muckraking
journalism. In the late 19th century, campaigns by newspapers in the US
to achieve reform were called “crusades”. We are perfectly aware that this
term has a repugnant connotation in the Arab world. The instructor may
simply note that in the Christian world, moral crusades have been
practiced at home as well as abroad, and are an accepted part of that
culture.


Nellie Bly’s “Ten Days in a Madhouse” was the cornerstone of one such
crusade, to reform asylums. Her story captures key elements of crusading
journalism, which marries facts with emotions, shocking revelations and
graphic, outraged language, leading to an explicit demand for reform. (This
is not only an American phenomenon. Albert Londres’s exposé of the
horrors at Devil’s Island, _Au bagne,_ followed the same template. It is a _3_
structural constant of popular journalism.)


Some of these crusades, such as the decades-long campaign to end
slavery, can be defended as necessary and just, despite the tragedy of the
American Civil War that followed it. Others, such as the crusade against
alcohol that led to Prohibition in the 1920s, were counter-productive;
Prohibition merely led to the emergence of rich criminal syndicates. Still
others, including the crusade of the Hearst newspapers to launch a war
against Spain in 1898, or the attempts by the Administration of George W.
Bush to justify an invasion of Irak by the presence of non-existent
weapons of mass destruction, were more or less cynical attempts to
manipulate public sentiment. A current crusade on CNN against human
trafficking displays two other weaknesses of the genre – a tendency to
make promises that cannot be kept, such as “ending human slavery,”
while claiming an undue share of credit for the network’s contribution to
ameliorating the problem. _4_


We would argue that mobilisation is at best an incomplete strategy for
investigative media that seek to reform society. To achieve reforms, it is
not enough for a journalist to outrage the public, or to humiliate leaders
through the revelation of unsolved problems. The first question that
individuals who are touched by a problem will ask, once they are informed,
is: “What can I do about it?” Journalists, editors and publishers cannot
answer that question alone. Nor can they ignore the possible
consequences of their crusades, as we will discuss below.


1 5 9 _3_
Londres, Albert. Au bagne. A. Michel, 1923.


_4_
See _http://thecnnfreedomproject.blogs.cnn.com/_


2.


Coalition. The great discovery of Protess et al. was that investigative
reporters achieve results most effectively when they form coalitions with
other social forces. Put another way, isolated reporters rarely prevail in
their search for reform or justice. Other actors must be engaged, both in
order to protect the reporter from reprisals, and to carry through a reform
agenda.


Some typical means of collaboration include:


a. Sharing sources, such as lists of contacts, or data, or access to witnesses.


Though investigative reporters must verify all of the information in their possession
before using it, they can also obtain documentation, insights or data from others.
Whistleblowers and NGOs are among the most common sources of such material.
Relationships with NGOs were crucial to the success of The EastAfrican (sic), the
weekly paper for the policy and business elites of the region that includes Kenya,
Tanzania and Uganda. Founding editor Joseph Odindo recalled that on issues such
as the health or economic impacts of outlawing or legalising the local drug, qhat,
NGOs “had all the information….


“They invest heavily in getting this information. The first reason we are not
able to accomplish investigations like the New York Times is that we don’t
have the skills, the capacity, the specialist writers. Two, we don’t have the
time; we can’t tie down a reporter for months.”


The instructor must be careful to remind students that investigative reporting does
not mean taking a report created by someone else and rewriting it. Like any other
source, the partners in a coalition must be regarded by a journalist as liable to error
and capable of manipulative tactics. Like any other document, the veracity and
authenticity of third party reports must be verified independently.


Nonetheless, this form of collaboration is growing. A common current form it may
take is for a NGO that has complied important data to share it with investigative
reporters. Recently, Greenpeace has adopted this strategy, even offering to pay
journalists to write reports, as they see fit, that use data compiled by Greenpeace.
Investigative journalism centres in different countries may also share information in
this way, in order to pursue international aspects of a story.


That is how the International Consortium of Investigative Journalists conducted its
landmark “Offshore Leaks” story. The story began when a journalist in Australia
came into possession of a hard drive containing millions of pages of raw data on
offshore bank accounts, involving thousands of individuals across the world. But the
data was effectively useless until a team of allied reporters in New Zealand, led by
Nicky Hager, figured out how to interpret it. At that point, teams in different
countries accepted the task of investigating individuals whose names appeared in the
files.  The coalition was later extended by making the full database available online, _5_
so that members of the public or judicial investigators could use it. _6_


_5_


_6_


_5_
The full project can be seen at : _http://www.icij.org/offshore_


1 6 0


_6_
See _http://www.icij.org/offshore/icij-releases-offshore-leaks-database-revealing-names-behind-secret-companies-trusts_


1 6 1


Frequently, investigative reporters will be asked, or pressured, to share information
with other media or official investigators after a story is published. Majdoleen Allain
faced demands from the religious court authorities in Amman to reveal which
officials had said that they accept bribes in order to do their work. Anne-Marie
Casteret was asked by a high-ranking inspector of the French Ministry of Health to
provide him with copies of documents that she had discovered during her four-year
investigation.


The instructor may ask students: _How should reporters answer such requests?_


In the examples above, Majdoleen Allain refused to reveal the names of her sources.
In contrast, Anne-Marie Casteret shared her documents with the inspector. The
instructor can ask: _What risks does sharing such information create for the reporter?_
_What risks will be reduced by sharing information?_


For Allain, revealing the names of her sources will expose them to sanctions, because
they told her the truth. Their only possible reason (besides the pain of guilt) for
telling the truth is that they, too, want the system to change. Thus sharing their
names will isolate the reporter from potential allies for reform, and damage them.
She therefore refused.


For Casteret, who began her revelations as the sole reporter in France with the
knowledge and sources required to understand and prove the story, the risk of
isolation was urgent and immediate. Educating the inspector to the facts offered the
possibility that her work would receive validation from a high-level official of the
State. If he chose to ignore the facts, her situation would be no worse than before.
Moreover, she had already published key information from the documents; her
adversaries would not learn more about her investigation through the inspector. She
therefore agreed to help the inspector.


_7_
See her Afterword in Casebook, pp. 48-49.


b. Sharing powers and rights


Different actors in a story may have powers that are not legally available to
journalists. For example, the plaintiffs in civil or criminal procedures, and their
lawyers, typically enjoy access to evidence that reporters cannot easily attain. The
late great editor Harold Brown formed a de facto alliance with the families of victims
in an airline crash in order to obtain evidence from the ongoing investigation; Nick
Davies of _The Guardian_ was directly inspired by this example when he forged a link
to lawyers for plaintiffs who sued the Murdoch empire for tapping their phones.
Anne-Marie Casteret, worked closely (though not exclusively) with victims in the
Contaminated Blood Affair to gather information, and also to prevent the official
inquiry into the affair from ending prematurely, before anyone was sanctioned for the
crime of selling AIDS-contaminated blood products. All of these examples show how
reporters, by working with victims, gain access to resources of the judicial system.


The history of investigative journalism also contains numerous examples of reporters
who collaborated with public officials who were eager to fight corruption. Clark
Mollenhoff’s distinguished career began in the provincial capital of Iowa City, rotten
with gangsters and bribe-taking politicians. Mollenhoff formed a team with an honest
prosecutor and an honest policeman. The prosecutor built cases; the policeman
arrested the crooks; and Mollenhoff provided leads and support through his
published reports. _8_


The key insight here is that reporters do not send criminals to jail. Police,
prosecutors, judges and juries do that. Reporters are thus well-advised to identify
honest people in each of these occupations, and to consider how they can cooperate
without compromising their ethics, reputations or objectives.


The instructor can ask: _What is the risk in such cooperation?_


One risk is very well-known. Reporters who seek to cooperate with public officials, or
business people, or social actors like NGOs, may view their role as enabling the right
people to triumph over adversity or indifference. Eventually, many such reporters
openly share in the power that emanates from a public figure, as a follower. In the
end, the reporter may be blinded by that power, and incapable of seeing when it
grows corrupt. An extreme example, common in France, involves women journalists
who report on the activities of their Minister husbands.


_8_
Clark R. Mollenhoff, Investigative reporting: From courthouse to White House. MacMillan, 1981.


_9_
See Gabler, Neal. Winchell: gossip, power, and the culture of celebrity. New York: Knopf, 1994.


1 6 2


There is no shortage of such examples in any country we know of. The instructor
may choose to evoke them by name, or not. What is important is to stress to your
students that their role as investigators is not to promote anyone. If they make
alliances with other actors in the society, those alliances must be temporary, with a
clear objective in mind. Once that objective is obtained, relationships with other
actors must remains arms-length.


c. Sharing the story


A fundamental insight of the coalition model, amply confirmed both by field
experience and research, is that an isolated reporter cannot succeed. The reasons
are quite practical. First, an isolated reporter can be safely ignored. Even if his or her
initial revelations are powerful, they will soon be forgotten in most cases. Unless the
reporter has compiled sufficient information to support a long series – for example,
when Anne-Marie Casteret published her first article in the Contaminated Blood
Affair, she had already planned a series of nine follow-ups using fresh information –
the story will become old news.


Equally important, a single reporter becomes very vulnerable. Pressure will be put on
editors and publishers to fire the reporter, or apologise for his or her story, or give the
reporter a new job cleaning out the same closet (or coffin) every day. If the reporter is
sued, judges and juries will note that no one seems willing or able to defend the
reporter’s revelations. Even if the case doesn’t get to court, the reporter may come
under attack by the targets of the exposé, their powerful friends, and their lackeys,
including consultants and journalists who wear a leash as if it were a diamond
necklace.


This was the fate of Gary Webb, an investigative reporter who reported in 1996 that
“for the better part of a decade, a San Francisco Bay Area drug ring sold tons of
cocaine to the Crips and Bloods street gangs of Los Angeles and funneled millions in
drug profits to a Latin American guerrilla army run by the U.S. Central Intelligence
Agency.”  Soon after the publication of his series “Dark Alliance” in the _10_ _San Jose_
_Mercury News_, the _New York Times_ and _Washington Post_ reported that they could not
substantiate Webb’s charges. Their reports were truthful, but had the effect of
isolating Webb without further investigation. The Mercury News apologised for his
story, and his career in the news industry effectively ended. His death at age 49 was
ruled a suicide. Following his death, other reporters confirmed parts of his story, and
his central argument – that the CIA had been aware of the traffic, and had protected
it – was considered “largely correct” by a scholarly observer who reviewed the case. _11_


_10_


_11_


1 6 3


_10_
Webb’s three-art series, “Dark Alliance”, can be found online at :
_http://www.narconews.com/darkalliance/drugs/start.htm_


_11_
F t M k (2008) C i Th i S d P i A i C lt ( i d diti )


It is not enough to be right. A wise investigative journalist will ensure, as well, that
he or she is not alone with the truth. In order to reduce this risk, the journalist
should ensure:


The facts of the story are not overstated or exaggerated. This was one reason
that Gary Webb found himself alone. According to his former editor, “He was
fundamentally a man of passion, not of fairness. When facts didn't fit his
theory, he tended to shove them to the sidelines."  Later on, when you begin _12_
project work with them, your students will be guilty of this. Please make clear
to them that by making statements that their evidence cannot support, they
cut themselves off from other actors.


The facts, and a viable path to the facts, must be open to other journalists.
Anne-Marie Casteret faced this issue at the moment she was about the reveal
the Contaminated Blood Affair; she knew that other reporters could not
duplicate her exploit in documenting the scandal. Her solution was to
distribute key documents to other media.


In contrast, even experienced reporters could not verify key elements of
Webb’s story, in part because it was based on the testimony of criminal
sources. (A favorite defense of criminals is to say that someone else is
responsible.) Again and again, Webb’s story leads to a dead end, which he
suggests is due to the CIA’s intervention; but that is not positive proof.


Social actors can be engaged, as sources and as potential allies, before the
story is published. The reporter does not need to seal a formal pact with
them. The reporter does need to seek their challenges, insight and
information, and to alert them that a story which can affect their objectives
and interests is coming. Students may ask how it is possible for them to
become expert enough to collaborate with activists or other interests, without
being dominated by them. The answer is that by investigating, they too will
become expert.


Equally important, in the contemporary era nearly all NGOs and other civil
society actors possess their own websites or other media. They can thus
provide support and publicity for an investigative story, whether or not other
mainstream media follow the case. In some instances, these “stakeholder
media”  can have a more powerful influence than mainstream media. _13_


_12_
Scott Herhold, “Thinking back on journalist Gary Webb and the CIA.” San Jose Mercury News, Feb.
10 2013, via _http://www.mercurynews.com/scott-herhold/ci_22560549/herhold-jeremy-renner-gary-webb-_
_movie-cia?IADID=Search-www.mercurynews.com-www.mercurynews.com_


_13_
See Mark Lee Hunter et al., Power is Everywhere: How stakeholder-driven Media Build the Future of Watchdog


1 6 4


B. Social forms of backlash against investigative reporting


We have already considered how American investigative journalists transformed
professional practice in the decades following Watergate. Another aspect of the story
deserves further discussion: The loss of public support for investigative reporters.
This phenomenon is distinct from official attacks on freedom of the press, and it is
recurrent in the history of investigative journalism. The Muckrakers of the early 20th
century attained the peak of their influence in the years 1906-1912, prior to the
election of a reformist President, Woodrow Wilson. In France, an investigative
reporting movement that began in the mid-1980s likewise lost influence and prestige
by the end of the 1990s.


These phenomena do not occur by accident. To a large extent, they result from
actions undertaken by adversaries of investigative reporting. (As Julian Assange of
Wikileaks once said, “Power pushes back.”) These actions include:


1. Cutting off advertising space purchases to punish a media.


This tactic aims to reduce the revenues available to a media. More than a century ago
it was employed against the weekly magazines that published the original
muckrakers, and it was employed against “alternative” weeklies in the _14_
post-Watergate era. It will be employed as long as media depend upon advertisers. It
can be highly effective, especially with media that lack a diverse and broad revenue
base.


The instructor can point out several solutions:


a.


b.


c.


Do not publish stories that offend advertisers on whom your media is
dependent. There are no doubt plenty of other stories that you can
publish.


Take the story to a media that is not dependent on the advertiser.


Since audiences tend to respect editorial independence more than
advertisers, seek to make circulation, donations or other revenues the
foundation of your media’s business model. This is the strategy adopted
by the _Canard enchaîné_, a French investigative and satirical weekly which
accepts no advertising but is nonetheless highly profitable. The _Canard_
derives about 50 million euros in annual revenue from a circulation based
of approximately 450,000 copies sold weekly. The fact that it carries no
advertising signals to the audience that the journal cannot be influenced
by advertisers.


1 6 5


_14_
there’s no text on the word file


2. Threatening editors or publishers with libel lawsuits.


Legal action against investigative reporters has gained in prominence since the
Watergate era, in the US and elsewhere. Whether or not a reporter has done a proper
job, the mere threat of such lawsuits can be highly dissuasive, because legal defense
is costly everywhere in the world. In the post-Watergate era, the great majority of libel
suits in the US – at least 60%, according to one study – were merely nuisance _16_
procedures without substance, aimed at harassing journalists who did a proper job.
Though the US Supreme Court generally protected the rights of journalists to tell
well-documented stories about the misdeeds of public figures, scholars documented
that editors and publishers were increasingly worried about the possibility of libel
suits following Watergate.  In the contemporary global era, this tactic has been used _17_
extensively by oligarchs from Eastern Europe who own residences or businesses in
the UK, where libel laws are quite favourable to plaintiffs and expensive to defend
against.  Likewise, in Kenya libel damages are often sufficient to bankrupt a journal. _18_


_16_


_17_


_18_


The instructor can greatly enrich this discussion by bringing a copy of his or her
country’s libel law to class, and helping students to discuss it. An exercise can be
prepared by obtaining a recent story that was the object of a libel lawsuit, and the
final judgment in the case.


The best examples will be those that illustrate clearly not only the sanctions against
reporters who commit libel, but the protections that libel law provides to reporters.
This will appear clearly in judgments for and against reporters.


In France, for example, there are two tests of whether libel (which is there called
“defamation”) has been committed.


The first is whether the information provided by a reporter is “complete and
coherent.”


The second is whether or not the reporter acted in good faith, by trying to
consider all sides of a story and verify the facts.


In the UK, there is another test:


Is there a strong public interest in publication?


In one form or another, these tests appear in nearly every jurisdiction in the world.
By knowing the legal tests that they must satisfy, reporters reduce their risks and
make it harder for plaintiffs to sue them.


Students may object that in their country, the courts are notoriously corrupt, and
judicial careers are made by pleasing the sort of powerful people who dislike
investigative reporting. That may be true. It is also true that nearly everywhere, those
in power are afraid of public opinion. They do not wish to undertake measures that
may lead to revolt, or even ridicule. Journalists are therefore well advised to make it
clear to their audiences that they are acting in the public interest, and not only tio
grow their own reputations.


_16_
Sam Riley, "Fighting Back: What Redress Media Have Against Frivolous Libel Suits."
Journalism Quarterly 59:566-572 (1982)

_17_
Reference


_18_
Kiev Post


1 6 6


3. The limits of adversarial reporting


The idea that reporters are a counter-power, a Fourth Estate, is deeply rooted in the
ethics and ideals of the profession. However, that role is very largely dependent on its
acceptance by the public, in more ways than one. If people do not believe that an
investigative reporter is trying to tell the truth about a matter of importance, they will
not help the reporter. If they believe that reporters are merely acting in a vicious or
vengeful way, they will grow afraid of them. (This is what happened to the _News of_
_the World_, and the ultimate result was a coalition of the media’s victims, including
celebrities, parliamentarians, journalists, and the parents of a murdered child whose
phone had been tapped. The coalition succeeded in destroying the newspaper’s
advertising base, and it folded immediately. ) _20_


In the late 1970s, investigative reporters appeared arrogant and aggressive to a large
share of the American public, as well as to their colleagues and leaders in the news
industry. Their denunciations of political, social and business leaders no longer
seemed exciting; they were merely depressing. One prominent scholar warned that
the unending stream of “bad news” could make both news consumers and reporters
mentally ill.  This was exaggerated, and one-sided: Allowing corruption to thrive can _21_
be extremely depressing, too. But Americans seemed to be weary of hearing that
their society was run by crooks and incompetents. In 1980, the election of a
conservative Republican, Ronald Reagan, to the US Presidency signaled the end of
the Watergate era, just as the election of Woodrow Wilson in 1912 had put an end to
the Progressive era. At New West magazine, a leading investigative journal of the
West Coast, owner _William Broyles_ advised his reporters that no one was interested
in their complaints. _22_


_21_


_22_


1 6 7


_20_
See Watson, Tom, and Martin Hickman. Dial M for Murdoch: News Corporation and the corruption
of Britain. Penguin UK, 2012.


_21_
Haskins, Jack. B., The Trouble With Bad News. ERIC Clearinghouse, 1981.


4. Official repression


The contemporary period is one of great struggle between governments and
journalists and other advocates of transparency and free expression. In Egypt, the
Morsi administration obtained the conviction of 43 NGO workers, including
Egyptians, Jordanians and Palestinians, on charges of working for unlicensed
institutions and receiving illegal funding in 2013.  Their sentences were maintained _23_
when the military overturned Morsi. China has targeted anti-corruption activists. _24_
The list of journalists murdered in Russia has become considerably longer during
Putin’s domination of the country. Even the United States, long considered a _25_
champion of freedom of expression, has lately become known for prosecution of
whistleblowers and fierce pursuit of those who reveal government secrets, like _26_
Julian Assange and Edward Snowden. It cannot be said when or how these trends
will abate.


_23_


_24_


_25_


_26_


Your students will ask: _Doesn’t all of this mean that investigative reporting is bad for_
_the news industry, and bad for our careers?_


Not quite. First, the adversarial tradition remains alive. Consider _The Intercept_, an
online news media created to expose the activities of the US National Security
Agency. This high-level website declares:


_Our long-term mission is to produce fearless, adversarial journalism across a_
_wide range of issues. The editorial independence of our journalists will be_
_guaranteed. They will be encouraged to pursue their passions, cultivate a_
_unique voice, and publish stories without regard to whom they might anger or_
_alienate. We believe the prime value of journalism is its power to impose_
_transparency, and thus accountability, on the most powerful governmental and_
_corporate bodies, and our journalists will be provided the full resources and_
_support required to do this.27_


In other words, official repression has not reduced the desire of journalists to “tell
truth to power”. Indeed, as we saw in Chapter Four, investigative journalism is
experiencing a global renaissance. Quite simply, the public wants journalists who
will defend society, especially when government seems unable or unwilling to do so.

More important, as a historical fact in every country we know of, what the public
rejects is investigative reporting aimed at accusations, not solutions. Investigative
journalists must not forget that the point of their work is to make the lives of others
better.


Their skills are in high and growing demand around the world. Investigative
reporters like Yosri Fouda in Egypt, Umar Cheema in Pakistan, and Miranda
Patrucic in Bosnia, and many others show that investigative journalism is still a way
for young reporters and new media to make a reputation and build a public.


_23_
http://www.al-monitor.com/pulse/originals/2013/06/ngo-workers-sentenced-egyptian-court.html#


_24_
http://www.voanews.com/content/china-puts-more-anti-corruption-activists-on-trial/1835904.html


_25_
A detailed account, and supplemental sources, can be found at http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
List_of_journalists_killed_in_Russia

_26_
http://www.salon.com/2012/02/09/obamas_unprecedented_war_on_whistleblowers/

_2_


1 6 8


1 6 9


Moreover, while the public may dislike an unending stream of adversarial
journalism, the public likes even less the empty “information” that took its place.
This may not be immediately evident in countries where liberalisation of the media
has allowed large numbers of entertainment media to flourish, crowding public
space with “people” journalism of little serious interest. (Such countries include
Italy, as well as many in the developing world.)


But in mature media markets, the impact of worthless news on the value of news
media appears more and more evident. From the 1970’s through the mid- 1980’s,
confidence in the press was as high as it was for other major institutions— the
military, Congress, religion and education, to name a few. But in the late 1980’s,
ratings for the press began to slip, and by the 1990’s the slip had become a slide. In
1990, 74 percent of Americans said they had a great deal or some confidence in the
press. A decade later, that number had fallen to 58 percent. During the same period,
confidence in other institutions remained stable. _28_


By the end of the first decade of the 21st century, no more than 23 % of the American
public considered any major news media wholly or largely credible, according to the
benchmark studies of the Pew Institute (which we urge the instructor to follow for
insight into trends in the crucial US news market).  It would be unscientific to _29_
declare that the post-Watergate era decline of investigative reporting in the US was
solely or wholly responsible for this fall in credibility. It would be perfectly
reasonable, however, to say that cutting back investigative reporting does not
appear, in any way, to make news media more credible. In an industry whose unique
selling proposition is the provision of valid information, that is a serous handicap. No
journalist will ever be free of that suspicion. But investigative reporters can be freer
than most.


_28_
Doherty, C., “The Public Isn’t Buying Press Credibility”. Nieman Reports, Summer 2005, via
_http://www.nieman.harvard.edu/reports/article/101115/The-Public-Isnt-Buying-Press-Credibility.aspx_
